Collective Ijtihad and Its Foundations in Shura
Argument for collective ijtihad with prophetic and caliphal evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an argument for collective ijtihad by citing the Quranic principle of shura, the hadith of 'Ali, and the practice of 'Umar, and evaluate its necessity in modern times.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad through shura is not a modern innovation but a Prophetic and Caliphal method revived by necessity.

In this passage, the author argues that the dramatic changes of modern civilization require a new form of ijtihad: collective ijtihad (ijtihad al-jama'ah) conducted through shura among scholars from all madhhabs. He grounds this proposal in the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, particularly 'Umar, and in direct Prophetic instruction. By examining these evidences, you will understand how classical methods can address contemporary legal challenges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Note the author's description of modern technological and social changes that render individual ijtihad insufficient. Locate the phrase 'صيانة الأحكام الاجتهادية' and the list of inventions.
This sets the maqasid context for the proposal.
2
Extract the proposed solution
The author proposes 'اجتهاد الجماعة' (collective ijtihad) on the method of 'الشورى العلمية' (scientific consultation) in jurisprudential assemblies. Identify the key terms.
3
Locate the first proof: Quranic shura
Find the verse 'وَأَمْرُهُمْ شُورَى بَيْنَهُمْ' (Surah al-Shura 38). Note how the author interprets 'أمر' to include scientific and political affairs.
This is the general principle.
4
Locate the second proof: Hadith of 'Ali
The Prophet ﷺ instructed 'Ali: 'اجمعوا العالمين من المؤمنين فاجعلوه شورى بينكم ولا تقضوا فيه برأي واحدة'. This directly commands collective deliberation. Note the chain and authentication.
This is a specific prophetic endorsement.
5
Locate the third proof: Practice of 'Umar
The author details 'Umar's public and private shura, especially the case of Sawad al-Iraq lands, where he gathered the Companions for days and they reached a collective decision prioritizing future generations.
This demonstrates practical application.
6
Synthesize the argument
Combine all three evidences: Quranic principle, prophetic command, and caliphal practice. Understand how they collectively support the necessity of collective ijtihad today.
The argument moves from general to specific to precedent.

Source Text

٣/١٤ - اجتهاد الجماعة :
هذا، وإذا كانت صيانة الأحكام الاجتهادية التي وجد منها ثروة فقهية كافية في الماضي قد اقتضت سد باب الاجتهاد الفردي، ففي هذا الزمن تغير وجه المصلحة بما جدَّ من عجائب الحضارة فقد سخر الإنسان فيه ما من قوى الطبيعة الكونية حتى طار في الهواء وغاص مسافراً ومحارباً تحت الماء، ثم حطم الآن الذرة واستخدم من قوتها ما حير العقول. وأصبح بفلكة زر من جهاز كصندوق صغير يرى فيه ويسمع من بلاد نائية وغزا الفَضَاء فأطلق الأقمار الاصطناعية تدور حول الأرض. ثم حقق حلمه بالرحلة إلى القمر، وأصبحت المواصلات بين مشارق الأرض ومغاربها تتم في سرعة المح البصر بقوة الكهرباء، وترابطت علائق الأمم الاقتصادية بعضها ببعض، وتلاقت قوانينها وجهاً لوجه. لذلك أصبح من مصلحة الفقه الإسلامي نفسه أن يقوم فيه اجتهاد من نوع آخر، هو اجتهاد الجماعة على طريقة الشورى العلمية في مجامع فقهية تضم فحول العلماء من مختلف المذاهب والأقطار، ليفوا حاجة العصر من هذا الفقه الإسلامي الفياض الذي لا ينْضَب معينه .
وهذه الشورى هي الطريقة التي كان يلجأ إليها الخلفاء الراشدون في
المشكلات العلمية والسياسية كلما حزبهم أمر .
وقد كان لعمر بن الخطاب شورى خاصة وشورى عامة. - فشوراه الخاصة كانت تختص بِعِليَةِ الصحابة من المهاجرين الأولين
وكبار الأنصار، وهؤلاء يستشيرهم في صغير أمور الدولة وكبيرها. - وأما الشورى العامة فقد كان يجمع فيها ذوي الرأي من أهل المدينة أجمعين في الأمر الخطير من أمور الدولة فيجمعهم في المسجد النبوي،
وإذا ضاق بهم جمعهم خارج المدينة، وعرض عليهم الأمر ورأيه فيه. فمن ذلك استشاراته لهم في أراضي سواد العراق عندما اختلف فيها مع رجال الجيش الفاتح الذين أرادوا اقتسامها كالغنائم المنقولة مما تقدَّم إيضاحه (ف ٤/١١). فقد جمع الصحابة وتناقشوا في ذلك، وتكرّر اجتماعهم يومين أو ثلاثة حتى استقرَّ الرأي على موافقة عمر في أنها فيء لا يقسم على الغانمين لأنه تتعلق بثمراته حقوق الأجيال القادمة أيضاً. (ر: مقدمة «الملكية ونظرية العقد للأستاذ أبي زهرة ف/ ۱۲ ص ۱۸). وأصل هذه الشورى في القرآن قوله تعالى عن المؤمنين:

3/14 - Collective Ijtihad: This, and if the preservation of ijtihad rulings (of which sufficient jurisprudential wealth was found in the past) necessitated closing the door of individual ijtihad, then in this time the aspect of maslahah has changed due to the wonders of civilization that have emerged. Man has harnessed the cosmic forces of nature, flying in the air and diving as traveler and warrior under water, then now shattered the atom and used its power to baffle minds. With the press of a button on a device like a small box, he sees and hears from distant lands, conquered space, launching artificial satellites orbiting the earth, then achieved his dream of traveling to the moon. Communications between the east and west of the earth occur in the blink of an eye by electric power. The economic relations of nations have become interconnected, and their laws have met face to face. Therefore, it has become in the interest of Islamic jurisprudence itself that a different kind of ijtihad should take place: collective ijtihad based on the method of scientific shura in jurisprudential assemblies that include prominent scholars from various schools and regions, to meet the needs of the era from this abundant Islamic jurisprudence whose source never dries up. This shura is the method that the Rightly Guided Caliphs used to resort to in scientific and political problems whenever an issue confronted them. 'Umar ibn al-Khattab had a private shura and a public shura. His private shura was specific to the elite of the Companions from the early Muhajirun and senior Ansar, and he would consult them in small and large state affairs. As for the public shura, he would gather all people of opinion from the people of Medina in serious state matters, assembling them in the Prophet's Mosque, and if they were too many he would gather them outside Medina, presenting the matter and his opinion on it. Among that was his consultation with them regarding the lands of Sawad al-Iraq when he differed with the conquering army who wanted to divide them as movable spoils, as explained previously (f 4/11). He gathered the Companions and they discussed it, meeting for two or three days until the opinion settled on agreeing with 'Umar that it was fay' (booty not divided) not to be divided among the combatants, because its fruits also concern the rights of future generations. The origin of this shura in the Quran is His statement about the believers: 'and their affair is [determined by] consultation among themselves', and 'affair' includes scientific and political matters. The Prophet ﷺ explained the method of shura in judicial matters: gathering knowledge, deliberating, and issuing a joint collective opinion. Malik ibn Anas narrated with his chain to 'Ali ibn Abi Talib that he said: I said: O Messenger of Allah, an issue arises that has not been revealed in the Quran and no precedent from you has come? He said: Gather the knowledgeable believers and make it a shura among you, and do not decide on it with a single opinion. (Reference: I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in by Ibn al-Qayyim 1/73-74). (Reported by al-Tabarani in al-Awsat, with reliable narrators as per al-Haythami in Majma' al-Zawa'id 1/178, and al-Suyuti authenticated it in Miftah al-Jannah as per Kanz al-'Ummal 4188. Also reported by Ibn 'Abd al-Barr in Jami' Bayan al-'Ilm 1/16 from Malik's chain, but not in al-Muwatta.)
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy argument advocating for collective ijtihad (ijtihad al-jama'ah) as a necessary development in the modern era. It begins by acknowledging that in the past, individual ijtihad was closed (the famous 'closing of the door of ijtihad') because a sufficient fiqh corpus existed. However, due to unprecedented technological and social changes—flight, submarines, atomic energy, telecommunications, space travel, global economics—new legal questions arise that require a different approach. The author proposes collective ijtihad through shura in international juristic councils representing all madhhabs. He grounds this method in the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, giving the detailed example of 'Umar's consultation on the lands of Sawad al-Iraq, which involved multiple meetings and resulted in a decision that prioritized future generations' rights. He cites the Quranic basis for shura (Surah al-Shura 38) and a specific hadith from 'Ali where the Prophet instructed him to gather knowledgeable believers and make it a shura, not a single opinion. This hadith provides direct prophetic support for collective ijtihad. The passage type combines historical analysis, maqasid reasoning, and textual evidence to justify a new form of ijtihad.

Key Terms
اجتهاد الجماعة (ijtihad al-jama'ah): Collective ijtihad by a body of qualified scholars from different madhhabs through shura
الشورى (al-shura): Consultation among qualified people to reach a collective decision

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What proof does the author provide from the Prophet ﷺ for collective ijtihad in judicial matters?
A) The narration of 'Umar's consultation on Iraq lands.
B) The hadith of 'Ali where the Prophet instructed him to gather knowledgeable believers and make it a shura.
C) The verse 'And their affair is consultation among them.'
D) The consensus of the Companions on closing ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
Collective ijtihad via shura is a method rooted in the Quran, Sunnah, and Caliphal practice, necessary to address modern legal challenges.

Shura as a Method of Collective Ijtihad in Hanafi Fatwa Practice
Describes shura as practiced by Umar and its legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of shura (collective consultation) in the development of ijma and apply it as a method for issuing fatwas on novel legal matters.

Core Idea
Shura transforms individual ijtihad into collective authority, forming the foundation of ijma.

The practice of shura during the caliphate of Umar ibn al-Khattab illustrates how early Muslim jurists addressed new legal problems without direct textual precedent. This passage from al-Zarqa describes Umar's consultative assemblies and the Prophetic instruction to gather scholars. For a mufti, shura provides a safeguard against individual error and a pathway to consensus (ijma). Understanding this historical method is essential for applying collective ijtihad in contemporary fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the historical practice
Distinguish between Umar's private and public shura councils. Note that the private council consisted of elite companions, while the public council included all men of opinion from Medina. Recognize that shura was used for both administrative and legal matters.
The example of the Sawad al-Iraq lands shows how shura resolved a major financial and legal dispute.
2
Examine the scriptural basis
Identify the Quranic verse (42:38) and the hadith narrated by Ali. The verse establishes shura as a characteristic of believers; the hadith gives explicit instruction for collective consultation when no direct revelation exists.
The hadith's chain is considered authentic by al-Haythami and al-Suyuti, giving it evidentiary weight.
3
Connect shura to ijma
The author states that this scholarly shura is the origin of ijma. Understand that ijma is a formal source of fiqh, but it arises from the process of collective investigation (tamhīṣ) and agreement (ittifāq).
In Hanafi usul, ijma is binding; shura is the method to achieve it.
4
Compare madhhab views
Note that the Maliki school adopted shura as a formal principle for modifying rulings when customs change. In contrast, the Hanafi school does not treat shura as an independent source but as a recommended practice leading to ijtihad or ijma.
This difference affects how a mufti approaches novel cases: Hanafi muftis may consult but are not bound to do so.
5
Apply to fatwa practice
When a mufti faces a new case (nāzila) with no clear textual ruling, the principle of shura suggests gathering qualified scholars to deliberate. The resulting collective opinion carries more weight and reduces the risk of individual error.
This aligns with the maxim: 'Ijtihad does not cease' – collective ijtihad ensures continuity.

Source Text

الفصل (١٤) الدور الخامس : ٣٥١ - ٦٥٠هـ
وقد كان لعمر بن الخطاب شورى خاصة وشورى عامة. - فشوراه الخاصة كانت تختص بِعِليَةِ الصحابة من المهاجرين الأولين وكبار الأنصار، وهؤلاء يستشيرهم في صغير أمور الدولة وكبيرها. - وأما الشورى العامة فقد كان يجمع فيها ذوي الرأي من أهل المدينة أجمعين في الأمر الخطير من أمور الدولة فيجمعهم في المسجد النبوي، وإذا ضاق بهم جمعهم خارج المدينة، وعرض عليهم الأمر ورأيه فيه. فمن ذلك استشاراته لهم في أراضي سواد العراق عندما اختلف فيها مع رجال الجيش الفاتح الذين أرادوا اقتسامها كالغنائم المنقولة مما تقدَّم إيضاحه (ف ٤/١١). فقد جمع الصحابة وتناقشوا في ذلك، وتكرّر اجتماعهم يومين أو ثلاثة حتى استقرَّ الرأي على موافقة عمر في أنها فيء لا يقسم على الغانمين لأنه تتعلق بثمراته حقوق الأجيال القادمة أيضاً. (ر: مقدمة «الملكية ونظرية العقد للأستاذ أبي زهرة ف/ ۱۲ ص ۱۸). وأصل هذه الشورى في القرآن قوله تعالى عن المؤمنين: وَأَمْرُهُمْ شورى بينهم والأمر يشمل الشأن العلمي والسياسي. وقد بين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم طريقة الشورى في الشؤون القضائية بجمع العلم ومداولتهم، والصدور عن رأي مجتمع مشترك. فقد روى مالك بن أنس بسنده إلى علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه أنه قال : قلت: يا رسول الله، الأمر ينزل بنا لم ينزل فيه القرآن، ولم تمض فيه منك سنة؟ فقال : اجمعوا العالمين من المؤمنين فاجعلوه شورى بينكم ولا تقضوا فيه برأي واحده (۱) (ر) إعلام الموقعين لابن القيم ٧٣/١ - ٧٤ ) . (۱) رواه الطبراني في الأوسط، ورجاله موثقون رجال الصحيح، كما قال الهيثمي المجمع ۱۷۸/۱ ، وصححه السيوطي في مفتاح الجنة كما في كنز العمال) (٤١٨٨). ورواه ابن عبد البر في جامع بيان العلم) (١٦:١) من طريق مالك، لكن في غير الموطأ.

الباب الثالث : أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
ولا ريب أنَّ هذا الرأي العلمي الذي يصدر عن الشورى المجتمعة والتمحيص والتحقيق المشترك يكون أضمن للصواب والمصلحة من الفردية .
وقد تقدَّم أن هذه الشورى العلمية في عهد الخلفاء الراشدين هي نشأ بها فعلاً مصدر الإجماع من مصادر الفقه الإسلامي.
وقد أخذ الاجتهاد المالكي بمبدأ هذه الشورى العلمية بين علماء كل : زمن من تعديل الأحكام الفقهية عندما يتبدل فيها عرف الناس ومقاصدهم.
وهذا هو اجتهاد الجماعة الذي نرى أنه لا يسوغ انقطاعه (1)
(1) انظر في هذا الموضوع المحاضرة التي كتبها ونشرها الأستاذ الكاتب المؤرخ الكبير رفيق بك العظم رحمه الله بعنوان «قضاء الجماعة وقضاء الفرد».

Chapter (14) The Fifth Era: 351-650 AH. Umar ibn al-Khattab had a private consultative council and a public one. His private council was specific to the elite of the Companions from the early Muhajirun and senior Ansar, and he consulted them in the small and large affairs of the state. As for the public council, he would gather the people of opinion from all the people of Medina for grave matters of state, gathering them in the Prophet's mosque, and if they were too many, he would gather them outside Medina, and he would present the matter and his opinion about it. Among that was his consultation with them regarding the lands of Sawad al-Iraq when he differed with the conquering army men who wanted to divide them like movable spoils, as explained earlier (Section 4/11). He gathered the Companions and they discussed that, and their meetings were repeated for two or three days until the opinion settled on agreeing with Umar that they were fay' (conquest property) not to be divided among the victors because the rights of future generations are also attached to its fruits. (Refer: Introduction to 'Property and Contract Theory' by Professor Abu Zahra, Part 12, p. 18). The basis of this shura in the Quran is His statement about the believers: 'and their affairs are by consultation among them' and the matter includes both intellectual and political affairs. The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, explained the method of shura in judicial matters by gathering knowledge and deliberating, and issuing a joint societal opinion. Malik ibn Anas narrated with his chain from Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: I said, O Messenger of Allah, a matter befalls us for which no Quran has been revealed and no precedent from you exists? He said: Gather the learned from among the believers and make it a shura among you, and do not decide it by a single opinion. (1) (Refer: I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in by Ibn al-Qayyim, 1/73-74). (1) Reported by al-Tabarani in al-Awsat, and its narrators are trustworthy, men of Sahih, as al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id (1/178), and al-Suyuti authenticated it in Miftah al-Jannah as in Kanz al-Ummal (4188). Also reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr in Jami' Bayan al-Ilm (1/16) via Malik, but not in the Muwatta. Third Chapter: Eras of the Development of Islamic Fiqh and its Characteristics. There is no doubt that this scholarly opinion that issues from collective consultation and joint scrutiny and verification is more guaranteed for correctness and benefit than individual opinion. It has preceded that this scholarly shura in the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs is indeed the origin of the source of consensus among the sources of Islamic fiqh. The Maliki school of ijtihad adopted the principle of this scholarly shura among the scholars of each time regarding the modification of fiqh rulings when the customs and objectives of the people change. This is the collective ijtihad which we believe its cessation is not permissible. (1) (1) See on this topic the lecture written and published by the great historian writer Rafiq Bey al-Azm, may Allah have mercy on him, entitled 'Judgment of the Group and Judgment of the Individual'. 208:

Commentary
This passage is a historical and legal description of the practice of shura (consultation) during the caliphate of Umar ibn al-Khattab and its role as a source of Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by distinguishing between Umar's private and public consultative councils, then gives a concrete example of the decision regarding the conquered lands of Iraq. He then traces the scriptural basis of shura in the Quran and a hadith of the Prophet instructing collective consultation among scholars in new legal matters. The passage emphasizes that such collective ijtihad is more reliable than individual opinion and notes that the Maliki school adopted this principle for adapting rulings when customs change. The author concludes that this collective ijtihad (ijtihad al-jama'ah) should never cease. This passage illustrates how shura historically led to the development of ijma (consensus) as a source of fiqh and provides a model for fatwa issuance through scholarly consultation.

Key Terms
شورى (shūrā): Collective consultation among qualified scholars to reach a legal or administrative decision.
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus of qualified jurists on a legal ruling after the Prophet's death.
اجتهاد جماعي (ijtihād jamāʿī): Collective ijtihad where multiple scholars investigate and decide on a legal issue together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith narrated by Ali ibn Abi Talib in this passage, what did the Prophet instruct when a matter arises with no Quran or precedent?
A) Decide by your own opinion with qiyas.
B) Gather the learned believers and make it a shura.
C) Refer to the caliph for a binding decision.
D) Wait for a revelation or a dream.

Key Takeaway
Shura is a recommended method for collective ijtihad that historically gave rise to ijma, and muftis should employ it for novel cases to ensure accuracy and community consensus.

Sultanic Orders and the Ruler's Authority in Hanafi Ifta
Explains the authority of the ruler to issue binding orders on ijtihad-based issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a ruler's order on an ijtihad-based matter is legally binding, provided it serves public interest and does not contradict definitive texts.

Core Idea
A ruler's order can temporarily elevate a weak opinion to the binding position for public interest.

Throughout Islamic history, the ruler's authority to issue binding decrees on fiqh matters has been recognized within certain limits. Ottoman sultans, for instance, issued orders on practical issues like statutes of limitation, making them enforceable. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi justification for such orders and their conditions, drawing on principles of public interest (masalih mursala) and temporal change of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in question
Determine the specific fiqh matter addressed by the sultanic order—here, the prohibition of hearing lawsuits after a specified period (taqadum). Separate the original ruling (permissible to hear after any time) from the new restriction.
The order does not change the underlying Sharia ruling but restricts its application.
2
Classify the basis of the order
Recognize that the order is based on ijtihad—it addresses a matter open to scholarly interpretation and does not contradict a definitive text. Confirm that the order serves an emergent public interest (maslaha).
The author explicitly states that such orders are limited to 'ijtihad-based' issues and must align with public benefit.
3
Apply the relevant Hanafi usul principles
Link the order to two principles: (1) masalih mursala (unregulated interest) justifies restricting a permissible act; (2) taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-zaman (change of rulings with change of times) allows temporary adjustments.
These principles are foundational in Hanafi ifta for adapting to changing circumstances.
4
Determine the binding effect on muftis and judges
Once the sultanic order is issued, it becomes wajib (obligatory) to respect and implement. Muftis must give fatwa accordingly, and judges must rule by it. The previously weak opinion becomes the operative (mufta bihi) position.
Refer to Radd al-Muhtar and the author's appendix for detailed evidence.
5
Identify limitations and exceptions
Note that the order's binding force is conditional: it cannot contradict definitive nusus (Qur'an, mutawatir Sunnah) and must be within the ruler's jurisdiction. If the order conflicts with a clear text, it is not binding.
The passage stresses 'non-contradicting definitive texts' as a key condition.

(۱) وقد فصلنا ذلك في ملحق هذا الفصل، وأوردنا عليه الشواهد.
Chapter (15) The Sixth Stage: 651-1286 AH. 15/6 - (Second matter) - Issuance of sultanic orders during the Ottoman era on some practical fiqh matters, such as prohibiting hearing lawsuits after a specified period, which is called prescription (taqadum), and other measures issued by sultanic orders. Islamic ijtihad has recognized for the general ruler, whether caliph or otherwise, to limit the scope of some legal rulings and their application, or to order acting upon a weak, preponderated opinion when temporal benefit requires it, so it becomes the preponderant opinion that must be acted upon. Our jurists have explicitly stated this, in accordance with the principle of public interest (masalih mursala) and the principle of change of rulings with change of times. Texts of jurists in various chapters indicate that if the ruler issues an order on a subject of ijtihad—i.e., subject to ijtihad, not contradicting definitive texts of the Sharia—his order is obligatory to respect and implement legally. For example, if he prohibits some contracts due to an emergent benefit that must be observed, even though those contracts were originally permissible and effective legally, they become, by virtue of his prohibition, void or suspended according to the order (see Radd al-Muhtar 1/55). (1) We have detailed this in the appendix to this chapter and provided evidence for it.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the second distinguishing feature of the sixth stage: the Ottoman sultans issued binding orders (iradat sultaniyya) on certain fiqh matters, such as statutes of limitation (taqadum) preventing lawsuits after a set time. The author explains that Islamic legal theory recognizes the authority of the ruler (wali al-amr) to restrict the application of some Sharia rulings or to mandate acting upon a weak opinion when public interest (maslaha) demands it. This is based on the principles of masalih mursala (unregulated public interest) and taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-zaman (change of rulings with change of times). The passage further states that if a ruler issues a command on an ijtihad-related matter that does not contradict definitive texts, it becomes legally binding upon all. For example, if the ruler prohibits a contract that is normally valid due to a temporary exigency, the contract becomes void or suspended. The author references Radd al-Muhtar (a Hanafi fatwa reference) and notes that he has elaborated with evidence in an appendix. This illustrates the evolution of fiqh in response to changing circumstances within the Hanafi framework.

Key Terms
الإرادات السلطانية (al-iradat al-sultaniyya): Sultanic orders or decrees issued by the Ottoman ruler on practical fiqh matters, which become binding when they relate to ijtihad-based issues and serve public interest.
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursala): Unregulated public interests; considerations of benefit for the community that are not explicitly sanctioned or prohibited by Sharia texts.
تبدل الأحكام بتبدل الأزمان (taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-zaman): The principle that rulings may change as circumstances change, a key maxim in Hanafi fiqh for adapting to new situations.

Checkpoint

A) It must be unanimously approved by all scholars.
B) It must concern a matter open to ijtihad and not contradict definitive texts.
C) It must be issued by a caliph only, not any ruler.
D) It must be limited to criminal matters.

Key Takeaway
A valid sultanic order on an ijtihad-based matter is binding in Hanafi fiqh, temporarily elevating a preferred opinion for public welfare, provided it does not contradict definitive texts.

The Sultan's Authority to Issue Binding Decrees in Ijtihadi Matters
Discussion of sultanic authority to limit or enforce rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that a ruler's order in ijtihadi matters is binding when based on public interest, and apply this to a new scenario.

Core Idea
A ruler's order can make a weak opinion binding if it serves temporal public interest.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses a crucial aspect of Hanafi legal theory: the ruler's authority to restrict or enforce juristic rulings through sultanic decrees. This power is recognized when the matter is open to ijtihad (i.e., not determined by definitive texts) and the decree serves a genuine public interest. The passage links this to the broader principles of al-masalih al-mursala (unrestricted public interest) and the maxim that rulings change with changing times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key concepts
Focus on the terms al-irādāt al-sulṭāniyya (sultanic decrees), al-ijtihādī (subject to ijtihad), and al-maṣāliḥ al-mursala (unrestricted public interest). Understand how they interrelate.
2
Trace the legal basis
The author states that Islamic ijtihad affirms the ruler's power to limit rulings or enforce a weak opinion. Note the reliance on two legal maxims: al-masalih al-mursala and taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-azman.
3
Examine the conditions
The ruler's order is binding only if the matter is ijtihadi (subject to ijtihad) and does not conflict with definitive texts. Identify the limits of this authority.
4
Apply the example
The passage gives the example of statute of limitations (taqadum) and prohibition of previously permissible contracts. Understand how a sultanic decree changes the ruling.
5
Consider the implications for fatwa
Recognize that once the ruler orders a certain practice, the mufti must follow that order, and the previous weak opinion becomes the operative ruling.

This passage explains the authority of the ruling authority (sultan) in the Hanafi school to adjust the application of juristic rulings when public interest demands. The Ruler's Authority to Restrict Mubah for Public Interest
Establishes principle and provides three proofs from Sunnah and Sahaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-tasarruf 'ala al-ra'iyyah manut bi al-maslahah' to identify when a ruler may temporarily restrict a permissible act, and evaluate the legitimacy of such restrictions using the three historical precedents from the Sunnah and the practice of the Companions.

Core Idea
A ruler may temporarily restrict mubah for public interest, but only for a pressing need that outweighs the individual's right.

In this lesson, we explore a key principle of Islamic governance: the ruler's authority to limit the scope of certain permissible actions or even to temporarily adjust obligatory duties when a genuine public need arises. This authority is not arbitrary; it is bound by Shariah standards and is exemplified by the Prophet's own actions and those of Caliph Umar. We will examine three classical precedents that demonstrate the operation of this principle and consider its limits.

Guided Path
1
State the principle and its condition
Identify the maxim 'Conduct towards the subjects is dependent upon public interest' (Majalla Art. 58). Note the condition: the ruler's action must aim at achieving the community's interests according to Shariah standards.
This is the foundation for all the examples that follow.
2
Analyze the first evidence: hima (protected lands)
The Prophet established a hima at al-Naqi' and later Caliph Umar added more lands for state livestock. This shows restricting access to otherwise permissible land is allowed when needed for public welfare. Note the deduction that the Prophet's action was an executive act, not permanent legislation.
This precedent illustrates restriction of property rights for public interest.
3
Analyze the second evidence: temporary prohibition of storing sacrificial meat
The Prophet forbade storing Udhiyah meat beyond three days due to the arrival of needy Bedouins. Once the need passed, the prohibition was lifted. This highlights the temporary nature of such restrictions and the obligation of obedience.
A clear example of a temporary restriction on a normally permissible act.
4
Analyze the third evidence: Umar's deferral of zakat during famine
During a general famine, Umar delayed collecting zakat on livestock until conditions improved, then collected two years' worth. This demonstrates that even an obligatory duty can be temporarily adjusted for hardship, without being abrogated.
Shows the principle can apply to obligations with flexibility.
5
Synthesize common conditions
Across all examples, note the common elements: the restriction is temporary, it serves a specific recognized public need, it does not violate definitive texts (e.g., zakat itself was not cancelled), and it is based on the ruler's legitimate authority (not personal whim).
These conditions are crucial for fatwa and public policy.

ملحق بالفصل الخامس عشر
سلطة ولي الأمر، مداها وتقييدها
۸/۱٥ - قلنا في ختام الفصل السابق (٦/١٥): أن الاجتهاد الإسلامي قد أقرَّ لولي الأمر فرداً كان رئيس دولة مثلاً) أو جماعة (مجلس نيابي (مثلاً) أن يحد من شمول بعض الأحكام الشرعية وتطبيقها أو يأمر بالعمل بقول ضعيف مرجوح، أو يمنع بعض العقود أو الأشياء المباحة أصلاً إذا اقتضت ذلك مصلحة طارئة، كل ذلك بشرط أن يكون الهدف من هذه التصرفات تحقيق مصالح الجماعة، بمعاييرها الشرعية، لأن من القواعد الشرعية : أنَّ التصرف على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة (م/٥٨).
و نورد فيما يلي عدداً من الشواهد على هذا المبدأ من السنة النبوية، ثم من فقه الصحابة، ثم من آراء متأخري الفقهاء.
٩/١٥ - :أولاً: جعل بعض الأراضي محمية على خلاف الأصل): من عادة الرؤساء والملوك في الجاهلية أن يحموا أراضي يمنعون الناس من الرعي والصيد فيها ليختصّوا هم بها. وقد أبطل الإسلام ذلك بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا حِمى إلا لله ولرسوله» (رواه البخاري). وقد حمى الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أرض النقيع وحمى عمر أراضي لم يحمها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لترعى فيها ماشية الصدقة وخيل الجهاد» وقد استنتج بعض كبار الفقهاء (ومنهم الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله) أنَّ ما حماه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الأرض لم يكن على سبيل التشريع الدائم بل كان تصرفاً منه بالإمامة رئاسة الدولة)، فيجوز للخليفة من بعده أن يحمي بعض الأراضي للحاجة ومصلحة الأمة. وهذا الحمي للمصلحة العامة هو من قبيل منع الناس أن ينتفعوا من أراض هي مباحة لهم شرعاً في الأصل، وهو نقطة الشاهد في موضوعنا هذا.
١٥ / ١٠ - ثانياً : النهي عن إدخار لحوم الأضاحي لأمر عارض. للمسلم أن يأكل من لحم أضحيته، ويدَّخر منها، ويهدي ويتصدق. لكن ثبت في السنة الصحيحة فيما روى الإمام مسلم وغيره، أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر في إحدى السنين أن لا يأكل أحد من لحم أضحيته بعد ثلاثة أيام. ولما سُئل في العام التالي عما إذا كان عليهم أن يمتنعوا كالعام الفائت أخبرهم أنَّ النهي إنما كان بسبب قدوم أعراب فقراء محتاجين في العام الفائت فأراد لهم أن لا يدَّخروا بعد ثلاث، بل أن يبذلوا للمحتاجين اللحوم المتبقية من أضاحيهم؛ ولا حرج عليهم الآن في أن يأكلوا ويتصدقوا ويدخروا كما يشاؤون الزوال سبب النهي. ووجه الشاهد هنا أنَّ الرسول الله بوصفه ولي أمر المسلمين، نهاهم لسبب طارىء عن أمر مباح في الأصل وهو ادخار لحوم الأضاحي، فوجبت عليهم طاعته.
١١/١٥ - ثالثاً : فتاوى سيدنا عمر في عام المجاعة : وقعت مجاعة عامة في جزيرة العرب خلال خلافة عمر رضي الله عنه، فأصدر خلالها حكمين مهمين أولهما أنه أخر جباية زكاة الماشية (من) الإبل والغنم ... إلخ) حتى تزول المجاعة، رفقاً بأصحاب المواشي، فلما زالت وهطل المطر وتوافر المراعى أخذ منهم زكاتين، عن العام الفائت والعام الحاضر.
Appendix to Chapter Fifteen: The Authority of the Ruler, Its Extent and Limitation. 8/15 - We said at the end of the previous chapter (6/15): That Islamic ijtihad has affirmed for the ruler, whether an individual (e.g., a head of state) or a group (e.g., a parliamentary council), to limit the inclusiveness of some legal rulings and their application, or to order acting upon a weak, preponderated opinion, or to prohibit some contracts or things originally permissible if a temporary interest requires it; all of that on condition that the goal of these actions is to achieve the interests of the community according to its Shariah standards, because among the Shariah principles is: 'Conduct towards the subjects is dependent upon public interest' (Majalla Art. 58). And we list below a number of evidences for this principle from the Prophetic Sunnah, then from the fiqh of the Companions, then from the views of later jurists. 9/15 - First: Making some lands protected (hima) contrary to the original rule. It was the custom of leaders and kings in the Jahiliyyah to protect lands, preventing people from grazing and hunting therein so that they could exclusively possess them. Islam abolished that by his saying, peace be upon him: 'No hima except for Allah and His Messenger' (narrated by Bukhari). And the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, protected the land of al-Naqi', and Umar protected lands that the Messenger of Allah did not protect so that the livestock of sadaqah and the horses of jihad could graze there. Some leading jurists (including Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him) deduced from the above that what the Messenger of Allah protected of land was not on the basis of permanent legislation but was an action of his as Imam (head of state), so it is permissible for the caliph after him to protect some lands for need and the interest of the nation. And this protection for public interest is of the type of preventing people from benefiting from lands that are originally permissible for them by Shariah, and that is the point of evidence in this subject of ours. 10/15 - Second: The prohibition of storing sacrificial meat for a temporary reason. A Muslim may eat from the meat of his sacrificial animal, store it, give gifts, and give charity. But it is established in authentic Sunnah, as narrated by Imam Muslim and others, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, ordered in one year that no one should eat from the meat of his sacrifice after three days. And when asked the following year whether they should abstain like the previous year, he informed them that the prohibition was due to the arrival of needy Bedouins in the previous year, and he wanted them not to store after three days but to give the remaining meat to the needy; and there is no restriction on them now to eat, give charity, and store as they wish once the cause of the prohibition ceased. And the point of evidence here is that the Messenger of Allah, as the ruler of the Muslims, prohibited them due to a temporary reason from a matter originally permissible, namely storing sacrificial meat, so obedience to him was obligatory. 11/15 - Third: The fatwas of our master Umar during the year of famine: A general famine occurred in the Arabian Peninsula during the caliphate of Umar, may Allah be pleased with him. During it he issued two important rulings. The first: he deferred the collection of zakat on livestock (camels, sheep, etc.) until the famine ended, out of kindness to livestock owners. When it ended, rain fell, and pastures became abundant, he took from them two zakats: for the past year and the current year.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational legal-philosophical principle of Islamic governance: the ruler (wali al-amr) has the authority to temporarily restrict certain permissible (mubah) actions, or to mandate acting upon a weaker juristic opinion, if a pressing public interest (maslaha) so requires. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, frames this as a recognized principle in Islamic ijtihad, with the key condition that the ruler's actions must be genuinely aimed at achieving the community's welfare according to Shariah standards. The principle is anchored in the well-known maxim: 'Conduct towards the subjects is dependent upon public interest' (Article 58 of the Majalla).

The passage then provides three historical evidences:
1. The practice of hima (protected lands): While the Prophet abolished the pre-Islamic practice of leaders reserving land for themselves, he himself designated al-Naqi' as a protected area. Later, Caliph Umar established additional protected lands for the benefit of the state's livestock. Scholars like al-Shafi'i concluded that the Prophet's action was not permanent legislation but an executive decision as head of state, thus later rulers may similarly restrict access to otherwise permissible lands when public need arises.
2. The temporary prohibition of storing sacrificial meat: The Prophet forbade storing meat beyond three days in a particular year due to the arrival of needy Bedouins. This demonstrates a ruler's authority to restrict a permissible act for a temporary reason; once the reason ceases, the restriction lifts.
3. Umar's deferral of zakat during famine: Umar postponed collecting zakat on livestock during a general famine to relieve hardship, then collected two years' worth later. This shows that even obligatory duties (zakat) can be temporarily adjusted by the ruler for public welfare.

All examples underscore that the ruler's limitation of generally permissible or obligatory acts is valid only when based on genuine public interest and is temporary by nature.

Key Terms
ولي الأمر (wali al-amr): The ruler or governing authority (individual or collective) who has the power to issue binding orders in the public interest.
حِمى (himā): A protected area where public access (e.g., grazing, hunting) is restricted for a specific public benefit, such as preserving resources for state needs.
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Public interest or welfare, evaluated by Shariah standards, that may justify temporary restrictions on mubah or even some obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must the ruler's restriction of a permissible act satisfy?
A) It must be permanent and affect all citizens equally.
B) It must aim at achieving the community's interests according to Shariah standards.
C) It must be based on the ruler's personal opinion or convenience.
D) It must be approved by a majority of jurists.

Key Takeaway
The ruler's authority to restrict mubah is not absolute; it is conditional on genuine public interest, temporariness, and respect for definitive Shariah rulings.

Ruler's Authority to Nullify Contracts: A Rasm Rule
Establishes a rasm rule on ruler's orders and refutes a misconception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi-based rule that a ruler's binding order issued for a valid public interest (maslahah) renders contracts void in shari'ah, and identify and refute the contrary misconception.

Core Idea
A ruler's order for maslahah makes contracts void both legally and religiously.

This lesson examines a critical rasm (established rule) in Hanafi ifta': when a ruler (wali al-amr) issues a prohibition on certain contracts for a legitimate temporary interest, those contracts are not only void in secular law but also in shari'ah. The passage from al-Zarqa refutes the common error that such contracts remain valid 'religiously' (diyanatan) despite the legal prohibition. We will explore the rationale (ta'lil) and apply the rule to two concrete cases: unregistered property sales and judicial divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core rule: The ruler's prohibition of a contract for a recognized maslahah renders it void in shari'ah (both qada'an and diyanatan).
The rule applies only when the order is explicit and serves a valid public interest.
2
Understand the Scope (Qada vs. Diyana)
Distinguish between the two dimensions: qada'an (judicially enforced) and diyanatan (religious obligation). The passage asserts the ruler's order affects both, eliminating the gap some imagine.
Normally, shari'ah might hold a contract valid religiously even if unenforceable in court, but the ruler's explicit nullification overrides that.
3
Recognize the Misconception
Identify the false claim: Some argue that since shari'ah only requires offer and acceptance, unregistered contracts remain valid in the sight of Allah even if the court rejects them. This is the error the passage refutes.
The refutation hinges on the ruler's authority to regulate transactions for maslahah.
4
Apply to Parallel Case (Divorce)
See the analogy: Similarly, when a court grants a judicial divorce at the wife's request under personal status law, the couple is completely separated religiously, not just legally.
The same principle applies: the ruler's order (through the judge) ends the marriage even if the husband still considers her his wife.
5
Derive the Ta'lil (Rationale)
Explain why the rule holds: The shari'ah delegates to the ruler the estimation of public interest (taqdir al-maslahah) in organizational matters like land registration. Thus, his order is binding and overrides the default permissibility of unregistered contracts.
This is an exercise of siyasah shar'iyyah within the Hanafi framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعتبر : هذه العقود خارج المكتب العقاري باطلاً، أو غير نافذة الأثر ـ يجب أن هذه العقود في نظر الشرع أيضاً هكذا باطلة أو غير نافذة على حسب النص القانوني، لأنَّ هذا المنع صَدَر عن ولي الأمر لمصلحة زمنية تنظيم السجل العقاري يعود إليه شرعاً أمر تقديرها . فلا مجال لما يتوهمه بعضهم من أنَّ هذه العقود العقارية تبقى صحيحة ونافذة شرعاً في الواقع، وأنه يجب تنفيذها ديانة وإن كانت باطلة أو غير نافذة قضاء بالنظر القانوني، بحجة أن الشرع لم يشترط في شيء من هذه العقود تسجيلاً، وإنما اكتفى بالإيجاب والقبول اللفظيين - ومثل ذلك يجب أن يعتبر في قضية التفريق بين الزوجين قضاء بطلب المرأة بناء على قانون الأحوال الشخصية لدينا فلا مجال لما يتوهمه بعضهم من أنَّ المرأة تبقى في الواقع ديانة على عصمة زوجها الذي قضى بالتفريق بينها وبينه .
Consider: These contracts outside the land registry are void, or without effect — it is necessary that these contracts in the view of the shari'ah also are thus void or ineffective according to the legal text, because this prohibition came from the ruler for a temporary interest of organizing the land registry, the estimation of which returns to him in shari'ah. So there is no room for what some imagine that these property contracts remain valid and effective in shari'ah in reality, and that they must be executed religiously even if they are void or ineffective judicially from a legal perspective, under the pretext that the shari'ah did not require registration in any of these contracts, and was content with verbal offer and acceptance. Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule regarding the authority of the ruler (wali al-amr). The author states that when the ruler issues an order prohibiting certain contracts (such as unregistered property contracts) for a legitimate temporary interest, those contracts are considered void in shari'ah as well, not just in law. The reasoning is that the prohibition is issued by the ruler for a maslahah, and the shari'ah recognizes the ruler's authority to regulate such matters. Therefore, the contracts are void nafidhan (judicially) and also diyanatan (religiously). The same principle applies to judicial divorces: the court's ruling separates the couple, and they cannot consider themselves still married religiously. The author refutes the misconception that such contracts or marriages remain valid in the eyes of shari'ah even if the court rules otherwise.

Key Terms
ولي الأمر (wali al-amr): The ruler or authority figure with the power to issue binding orders for public interest.
قضاءً (qada'an): Judicially; in terms of court enforcement.
ديانةً (diyanatan): Religiously; in terms of obligation before Allah.
مصلحة زمنية (maslahah zamanah): A temporary public interest that arises due to specific circumstances.

The Authority of the Ruler and Its Limitation: Obedience Only in Ma'ruf
Raises a critical objection to ruler's authority and implies its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the potential problem of granting rulers unrestricted authority over ijtihadi rulings and apply the Hanafi principle that obedience is conditional on conformity to shari'ah.

Core Idea
Ruler's authority over ijtihadi rulings is limited: obedience only when the order does not violate shari'ah.

In earlier discussions, we established that a ruler can regulate ijtihadi matters in the public interest. However, this raises a serious concern: what if the ruler is corrupt or ignorant? This passage confronts that problem head-on. The implicit answer, grounded in the maxim 'la ta'ata li makhluq fi ma'siyat al-Khaliq', sets an essential limit on the ruler's authority. The mufti must evaluate each order: if it contradicts a clear text or involves sin, it is not to be obeyed.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the risk
Identify the passage's warning: a ruler may abuse authority out of whim, ignorance, or corruption, potentially undermining the shari'ah.
2
Recall the foundational maxim
The Hanafi school holds that 'No obedience to a creature in disobedience to the Creator' (la ta'ata li makhluq fi ma'siyat al-Khaliq). This sets the boundary for ruler's authority.
This maxim is universally accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the maxim to the objection
If the ruler issues an order that contradicts a nass or involves sin, it is not binding. Obedience is only required in ma'ruf (that which is good and lawful).
4
Identify the mufti's role
The mufti must evaluate each ruler's order against the shari'ah. If it conflicts, he must clarify the correct ruling to the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي أن يقال: إنَّ إعطاء هذه الصلاحية لولي الأمر العام يؤدي إلى إمكان أن يتصرف هذا الحاكم بحسب هواه في تغيير الأحكام الاجتهادية وتقييدها بأوامر أو قوانين زمنية يصدرها وقد لا يهمه موافقتها لقواعد الشريعة، وقد يكون هو جاهلاً أو فاسقاً لا يبالي تهديم الشريعة. فكيف تجب طاعته شرعاً في هذه الأوامر؟

So how can obedience to him be obligatory in these orders?

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental problem: if we grant rulers the authority to regulate ijtihadi matters (as discussed earlier), there is a risk that they may issue orders based on personal whim, ignorance, or corruption, potentially undermining the shari'ah. The author questions how obedience can be obligatory in such cases. The implied answer, derived from earlier discussions and the principle that 'obedience is only in what is right' (la ta'ata li makhluq fi ma'siyat al-Khaliq), is that the ruler's orders are only binding if they conform to the shari'ah. The mufti must evaluate each order: if it contradicts a clear text or involves sin, it is not to be obeyed. This sets a limitation on the earlier principle of the ruler's authority.

Key Terms
ولي الأمر العام (wali al-amr al-'amm): The general ruler or sovereign with overall authority over the Muslim community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Distinguishing Substantive and Procedural Laws
Legal philosophy exposition on law classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam) and procedural laws (qawanin al-marasim), and explain why procedural laws are necessary and changeable while substantive laws are grounded in fixed Islamic fiqh principles.

Core Idea
Procedural laws are administrative, changeable measures; substantive laws derive from fixed Islamic fiqh principles.

This passage by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa categorizes laws into two types: those that determine the legal ruling of an action, and those that prescribe the method for legal processes. He argues that the latter are indispensable but flexible, while Islamic fiqh provides comprehensive substantive principles. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti who must apply both fixed rulings and procedural regulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and find where the author defines ‘قوانين الأحكام’ and ‘قوانين المراسم’. Note the Arabic terms and their English equivalents.
The author also calls procedural laws ‘القوانين الشكلية’ (formal laws).
2
Analyze the examples
Examine the example given: selling a minor's real estate involves a substantive ruling (permissibility), while the requirement to register the contract in a real estate office is procedural. Practice classifying similar scenarios.
The same act can involve both substantive and procedural dimensions.
3
Understand the necessity argument
Identify why the author claims procedural laws are indispensable: they are based on legislative policy and can change over time, unlike substantive principles rooted in justice and rights.
The author stresses that procedural laws are not derived from fixed principles but from administrative expediency.
4
Recognize the self-sufficiency of Islamic fiqh
Note the list of principles that make Islamic fiqh sufficient for substantive laws: establishing truth, preventing harm, respecting contracts, etc. Consider how these principles can address new issues.
The passage mentions that Islamic fiqh includes principles like ‘al-umur bi-maqasidiha’ and ‘la darar wa la dirar’ implicitly.
5
Apply the distinction to contemporary scenarios
Given a modern legal question, decide whether it falls under substantive or procedural law. For example, is the requirement to use a specific contract form substantive or procedural?
This skill helps a mufti determine when rulings are immutable and when they can be adapted.

الزمنية يحوّلون ويبدلون من مجاريه دائماً على حسب ما يرون أنه أصلح وأضبط. فقد يوجبون اليوم طريقة في توثيق العقود أو تقديم الدعاوى إلى الحكام، أو تسجيل الأحكام أو تنفيذها، ثم يرون غداً ما وأحوط وأفضل، فيعدلون إليه. هو أسهل وقد يعينون للتقادم مرور) (الزمن مدة ثم يرون المصلحة في زيادتها أو نقصها دون أن تتبدل أو تتغيَّر المبادىء والنظريات الحقوقية التي تحكم في أصل الحق. ولذلك لم يأت التشريع الإسلامي في أصله بشيء من أحكام المراسم إلا ما اعتبر فيه مصلحة ثابتة دائمة كلزوم الإشهاد في عقد النكاح، وطريقة التلاعن بين الزوجين عندما يتّهم الرجل امرأته بالزنى وكالإرشاد العام إلى توثيق العقود بالكتابة أو الشهادة أو الرهن وكأصول القضاء الأساسية من استماع الدعوى، وسؤال الخصم، وتكليف البينة أو اليمين.

وأما ما يجد حدوثه في التعامل مما لم يكن موجوداً، كشركات
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics. A single madhhab, no matter how comprehensive, cannot fulfill all temporal needs and evolving interests that may lose their legislative treatment in that madhhab. And it exists in other ijtihads. 7/16 - The opinion on the necessity of this codification and its lack alongside fiqh: This, and the laws created by those factors and exigencies, like all laws that can exist for us or others, are of two types: First: substantive laws. They are those that impose and establish for every statement or action its legal consequence and ruling. Second: procedural or fundamental laws. They are those that outline and explain the methods that must be followed, and the forms according to which work must be issued, and are also called 'formal laws'. So, for example, the permissibility or impermissibility of selling a minor's real estate falls under substantive laws. As for the necessity of executing his contract in the real estate office and registering it in its register, it falls under procedural laws. Also of this kind is the law of court procedures, today also called 'law of pleadings', for its rulings are formal rulings. 8/16 - And we conclude from this an opinion that we see regarding the relationship between Islamic fiqh and codification, and we say: Procedural laws are an absolute necessity because they are not based on fixed principles of rights and rules of justice, but rather are measures related to legislative policy and the organization of its application more than they relate to those principles and rules. And this organization is a right of the public authorities and within their jurisdiction. Chapter (16) The Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH. Over time, they continually change and alter its courses according to what they see as most proper and precise. So today they may mandate a method for documenting contracts or presenting lawsuits to judges, or recording judgments or executing them, then tomorrow they see what is easier and more cautious and better, and they change to it. And they may assign a period for prescription by the passage of time, then they see the interest in increasing or decreasing it, without the legal principles and theories that govern the original right changing or altering. Therefore, Islamic legislation originally did not bring anything from procedural rulings except what was considered a permanent fixed interest, such as the necessity of witnessing in a marriage contract, the method of mutual imprecation between spouses when a man accuses his wife of adultery, and general guidance to document contracts by writing, witnessing, or pledge, and the basic principles of judiciary such as hearing the lawsuit, questioning the opponent, and requiring evidence or oath. 9/16 - As for substantive laws, Islamic legislation has made independent of any fundamental borrowing from another nation and foreign legislation, due to the broad horizon of Islamic fiqh with its various general rules and fixed foundations in which the idea of establishing truth and eliminating falsehood, repelling evils and bringing benefits, respecting wills and contracts, prohibiting coercion and nullifying its consequences, removing harm, considering necessities, responsibility for causation, diversifying and distributing guarantees with balanced justice, and considering custom and habits in contracts, transactions, and obligations, and other essential principles abundant and sufficient in Islamic legislation and its fiqh. And this Islamic fiqh has been a field for extensive ijtihads within the limits of Sharia, which we have indicated previously, and they are countless and their living opinions and theories never fall short of any legislative need, as we will soon see. And as for what arises in dealings that did not exist before, such as companies
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition discussing the relationship between Islamic fiqh and codification (taqnin). The author begins by noting that a single madhhab cannot fulfill all temporal needs, implying the need for ijtihad from other schools. He then classifies laws into two types: substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam) which determine the legal ruling of an action, and procedural laws (qawanin al-marasim) which outline the methods and forms for legal processes. These are also called formal laws. Examples are given: the ruling on selling a minor's property is substantive; the requirement to record the contract in a real estate office is procedural. The author argues that procedural laws are indispensable because they are administrative measures that can change over time based on expediency, while substantive laws are rooted in fixed principles of Islamic fiqh such as justice, harm prevention, custom, and others. He notes that Islamic legislation originally included only a few permanent procedural rulings (e.g., witnessing marriage, mutual imprecation, guidance on documentation). For substantive laws, Islamic fiqh is self-sufficient, containing comprehensive principles that can address any legislative need, including new matters like modern companies. The passage thus advocates for the adequacy of Islamic fiqh in substantive matters while acknowledging the necessity of procedural regulations that may adapt over time.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): Codification: the process of compiling and systematizing laws into a formal code.
قوانين الأحكام (qawanin al-ahkam): Substantive laws that establish the legal ruling of an action or statement.
قوانين المراسم (qawanin al-marasim): Procedural or formal laws that dictate the methods and forms for legal processes.
القوانين الشكلية (al-qawanin al-shakliyya): Formal laws, synonymous with procedural laws.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They are only needed in non-Muslim countries to organize courts.
D) They replace substantive laws when they are unclear.

Key Takeaway
Substantive laws are rooted in fixed Islamic fiqh principles, while procedural laws are administrative measures that can and should adapt to changing times.

Flexibility of Procedural Laws and the Richness of Substantive Principles
Distinguishing procedural from substantive law flexibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between procedural and substantive laws in Hanafi fiqh and explain why procedural rules are flexible while substantive principles are comprehensive and self-sufficient.

Core Idea
Procedural laws change with need; substantive laws need no borrowing.

Have you ever wondered whether Islamic law can adapt its court procedures or documentation methods to modern circumstances? In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies a crucial distinction: procedural laws (qawanin al-marasim) are designed to be flexible, adjustable by authorities as needed, while the substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam) are so rich and rooted in general principles that they require no external borrowing. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for the Hanafi tradition of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of law
Read the first few lines to find the two terms: procedural laws (qawanin al-marasim) and substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam). Understand that the author is contrasting them.
Procedural laws govern how cases are filed, documented, and executed; substantive laws define rights and obligations.
2
Extract the basis for flexibility of procedural laws
Locate where the author states that procedural laws are not fixed by revelation except for a few constant rules (like witnessing marriage, mutual imprecation, and basic judicial principles). The rest can be adjusted according to benefit.
The author gives examples: methods for documenting contracts, prescribing periods, recording judgments.
3
Recognize the fixed procedural elements
Note the specific exceptions: witnessing in marriage, mutual imprecation, general guidance for documentation, and basic court procedures (hearing, questioning, evidence). These are considered permanently beneficial.
These few rules are constant because they have inherent, unchanging benefit.
4
Understand the self-sufficiency of substantive laws
The author argues that Islamic substantive law is so rich in general principles (e.g., establishing truth, preventing harm, respecting contracts, considering custom) that it never needs to borrow from other legal systems.
The list of principles shows the breadth of fiqh: it covers justice, necessity, custom, liability, etc.
5
Apply to Hanafi jurisprudential practice
Consider how a Hanafi mufti would use this distinction: procedural rules can be changed via siyasa shar'iyya or urf, but substantive rulings are derived from the usul and are authoritative until a stronger proof appears.
This distinction empowers muftis to adapt procedures without compromising core principles.

الفصل (١٦) الدور السابع : ١٢٨٧ - ١٣٥٤هـ الزمنية يحوّلون ويبدلون من مجاريه دائماً على حسب ما يرون أنه أصلح وأضبط. فقد يوجبون اليوم طريقة في توثيق العقود أو تقديم الدعاوى إلى الحكام، أو تسجيل الأحكام أو تنفيذها، ثم يرون غداً ما هو أسهل وأحوط وأفضل، فيعدلون إليه. وقد يعينون للتقادم مرور الزمن مدة ثم يرون المصلحة في زيادتها أو نقصها دون أن تتبدل أو تتغيَّر المبادىء والنظريات الحقوقية التي تحكم في أصل الحق. ولذلك لم يأت التشريع الإسلامي في أصله بشيء من أحكام المراسم إلا ما اعتبر فيه مصلحة ثابتة دائمة كلزوم الإشهاد في عقد النكاح، وطريقة التلاعن بين الزوجين عندما يتّهم الرجل امرأته بالزنى وكالإرشاد العام إلى توثيق العقود بالكتابة أو الشهادة أو الرهن وكأصول القضاء الأساسية من استماع الدعوى، وسؤال الخصم، وتكليف البينة أو اليمين. ٩/١٦ - أما قوانين الأحكام فقد أغنى التشريع الإسلامي عن كل اقتباس أساسي فيها عن أمة أخرى وتشريع غريب بما في الفقه الإسلامي الواسع الأفق من القواعد العامة المتنوعة والأسس الثابتة التي رسخت فيها فكرة إحقاق الحق وإزهاق الباطل، ودرء المفاسد وجلب المصالح واحترام الإرادات والعقود ومنع الإكراه وإسقاط ما يترتب عليه، وإزالة الضرر واعتبار الضرورات ومسؤولية التسبب وتنويع وتوزيع الضمانات بعدل متوازن واعتبار العرف والعادات في العقود والأعمال والالتزامات، إلى غير ذلك من المبادىء الأساسية الكثيرة المغنية في التشريع الإسلامي وفقهه . وقد كان هذا الفقه الإسلامي ميداناً لاجتهادات واسعة النطاق ضمن حدود الشريعة قد أشرنا إليها فيما سلف وهي لا تحصى عدداً ولا تضيق آراؤها ونظرياتها الحية عن حاجة تشريعية أبداً، كما سنرى قريباً.
Chapter (16) The Seventh Era: 1287-1354 AH. The procedural laws change and alter their courses constantly according to what they see as more suitable and more precise. So they may today require a method for documenting contracts, presenting lawsuits to judges, recording judgments, or executing them, then tomorrow they see what is easier, more cautious, and better, so they change to it. And they may set a period for prescription, then see benefit in increasing or decreasing it without the legal principles and theories that govern the essence of the right changing. Therefore, Islamic legislation did not originally come with any procedural rulings except those in which a permanent and constant benefit was considered, like the necessity of witnessing in the marriage contract, the method of mutual imprecation between spouses when a man accuses his wife of adultery, and general guidance to document contracts by writing, testimony, or pledge, and the basic principles of judiciary like hearing the lawsuit, questioning the opponent, and requiring evidence or oath. 9/16 - As for substantive laws, Islamic legislation is independent of any fundamental borrowing from any other nation or foreign legislation due to the vast and varied general principles and fixed foundations in Islamic jurisprudence, which have firmly established the idea of establishing truth and eradicating falsehood, preventing evils and bringing benefits, respecting wills and contracts, preventing coercion and nullifying its consequences, removing harm, considering necessities, liability for causation, diversifying and distributing guarantees with balanced justice, considering custom and habits in contracts, actions, and obligations, among many other fundamental principles sufficient in Islamic legislation and its jurisprudence. And this Islamic jurisprudence has been a field for wide-ranging ijtihad within the limits of Sharia, which we have mentioned previously, and they are countless and their living opinions and theories never fall short of any legislative need, as we shall see shortly.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam discusses the nature of procedural laws (qawanin al-marasim) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author explains that procedural laws are flexible and can be changed by authorities according to what is more suitable and precise, as long as the underlying legal principles remain unchanged. Examples include methods for documenting contracts, court procedures, and prescription periods. These procedural matters are not fixed by revelation except for a few constant rules like witnessing in marriage and mutual imprecation. In contrast, substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam) are so rich in Islamic jurisprudence that they need no borrowing from other legal systems. The principles enumerated—such as establishment of truth, prevention of harm, consideration of custom, and necessity—form a comprehensive foundation for legislation. The passage emphasizes that Islamic fiqh has sufficient principles to address any legislative need through ijtihad, as will be seen in subsequent discussions.

Key Terms
قوانين المراسم (qawanin al-marasim): Procedural laws, i.e., regulations concerning court procedures, documentation, and formalities that can change based on circumstances.
قوانين الأحكام (qawanin al-ahkam): Substantive laws, i.e., the core legal rulings and principles that govern rights and obligations.
التلاعن (al-tala'un): Mutual imprecation (li'an) — a specific procedure when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are substantive laws (قوانين الأحكام) not in need of borrowing from other legal systems?
A) Because they are derived directly from the Quran and Sunnah without any interpretation.
B) Because Islamic jurisprudence contains a comprehensive set of general principles and foundations that can address any legislative need.
C) Because later Muslim jurists adopted Roman law, making borrowing unnecessary.
D) Because substantive laws were revealed all at once and never changed.

Key Takeaway
Procedural laws in Hanafi fiqh are flexible and can be adapted for public benefit, while substantive laws are built on comprehensive principles that need no external borrowing.

The Borrowed Mortgage: A Case Study in Applied Hanafi Qawa'id
Explaining conflicting elements and Hanafi derivation of ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conflicting elements in a borrowed mortgage and explain how Hanafi jurists harmonize them using multiple legal maxims.

Core Idea
Hanafi jurists resolve conflicting principles by deriving rulings from multiple qawa'id to balance all parties' rights.

The borrowed mortgage (الرهن المستعار) is a unique legal construct where an item is borrowed not for use but to be pledged as collateral. It combines two contradictory contracts: a non-binding gratuitous loan and a binding secured mortgage. This lesson explores how Hanafi jurists reconciled these tensions using a network of legal maxims, demonstrating the practical application of qawa'id in novel cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting elements
List the opposing characteristics of a loan (عارية: non-binding, trust, revocable) and a mortgage (رهن: binding, guarantee, irrevocable) as stated in the passage.
2
Recognize the need for resolution
Understand that these contradictions make the borrowed mortgage a problematic institution that requires juristic reconciliation to protect the rights of the borrower, lender, and creditor.
The passage mentions 'precision, benefit, and preservation of the rights of the three parties'.
3
Identify the applicable qawa'id
Although not explicitly listed, infer that the Hanafi jurists applied maxims such as 'الضرر يزال' (harm must be removed), 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative), and 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (matters are judged by intentions) to balance the conflicting principles.
The passage states rulings were derived 'من قواعد شتى' (from various principles).
4
Synthesize the ruling
Combine the maxims to produce a coherent ruling: the borrowed mortgage is valid and binding on the borrower, but the lender's right to revoke the loan is suspended as long as the mortgage is in effect, ensuring the creditor's security.
This synthesis illustrates talfiq of qawa'id.

C - The borrowed mortgage is that a person borrows something from his friend not to use it, but to pledge it as security and obtain a loan from it. It is established that a loan is a gratuitous contract that is not binding; the lender may cancel it whenever he wishes, while a mortgage is binding in order to protect the right of the creditor. Also, a loan is a trust that is not guaranteed if destroyed, while a mortgaged item is guaranteed. Therefore, the borrowed mortgage involves conflicting legal requirements. However, Hanafi jurists derived rulings for it from various principles, containing precision, benefit, and preservation of the rights of the three parties involved. (See: al-Majalla, article 372). (1) Such as hukr, ijaratayn, marsad, kadik, khulu, etc., which are well known in their place in the rulings of awqaf. The explanation of the meanings of these real rights established on awqaf will come in the third part of this series entitled: al-Madkhal ila Nazariyyat al-Iltizam al-‘Amma fi al-Fiqh al-Islami, in the discussion of types of real rights; so refer there, paragraphs 4/6 to 4/7.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage discusses a unique legal institution: borrowing an item not for use but to pledge it as collateral for a loan. This creates conflicting principles: a loan (عارية) is a non-binding, gratuitous contract that the lender can revoke anytime; a mortgage (رهن) is binding to secure the creditor's right. Moreover, a loan is a trust (أمانة) not guaranteed against destruction, while a mortgaged item is guaranteed (مضمون). The Hanafi jurists resolved these contradictions by deriving rulings from multiple legal maxims to balance the interests of the borrower, lender, and creditor. The footnote references similar rights over waqf properties like hukr, ijaratayn, etc., which will be explained in a later volume.

Key Terms
الرهن المستعار (al-rahn al-musta'ar): Borrowing an item not for use but to pledge it as collateral for a loan
الإعارة (al-i'arah): A gratuitous loan of an item for use, non-binding and revocable

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurists resolve conflicting contract principles by deriving rulings from multiple legal maxims to protect all parties' rights.

The Scope of the Majallah: Excluded Areas and Legal Codification
Describing the Majallah's nature and the three excluded areas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three areas of fiqh excluded from the Majallah and explain why each was excluded, and evaluate the significance of this exclusion for modern Islamic legal codification.

Core Idea
The Majallah codified only civil transactions, excluding worship, personal status, and criminal law as distinct spheres.

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah was a landmark in Islamic legal history as the first codification of civil law based on Hanafi fiqh. However, it intentionally left out three major areas of fiqh: acts of worship (ibadat), personal status (ahwal shakhsiyyah), and criminal law (uqūbāt). Understanding why these were excluded helps us grasp the practical scope of the Majallah and the considerations that guided early modern Islamic legal codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the Majallah
Understand that the Majallah was a civil code derived from Hanafi fiqh, intended to serve modern judicial needs.
2
List the three excluded sections
From the passage, note: ibadat, personal status, criminal punishments.
3
Explain the reason for each exclusion
Ibadat: not judicial; personal status: mix of spiritual and judicial; criminal: separate penal code.
4
Assess the impact on legal practice
Think about how this separation influenced later codification and the role of the mufti versus the judge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦ / ١٣ - وصف إجمالي للمجلة : أ - المجلة في الحقيقة هي أول تجربة رسمية لقانون مدني بالمعنى الحديث لكلمة القانون المدني) مأخوذ بكامله من الفقه الإسلامي. فقبلها لم يكن في الدولة العثمانية - وما يتبعها من البلاد العربية كسورية وفلسطين والعراق وسواها - قانون مدني عام، بل كان القضاء فيها يجري على الصحيح الراجح من آراء فقهاء المذهب الحنفي الذي هو مذهب الدولة .

16/13 - General description of the Majallah: A - The Majallah is in reality the first official experiment in civil law in the modern sense of the word 'civil law', taken entirely from Islamic fiqh. Before it, in the Ottoman Empire and its dependent Arab countries like Syria, Palestine, Iraq, etc., there was no general civil law; rather, the judiciary proceeded according to the correct and preponderant opinions of the Hanafi jurists, which was the state's madhhab. B - Three sections of fiqh were excluded from the Majallah: 1. Acts of worship (ibādāt), because the purpose of compiling the Majallah was to serve the judiciary and new judges and to ease the difficulty of fiqh reference for them in matters not covered by exceptional laws, especially commercial law. 2. Personal status from marriage to inheritance and everything between, because they involve many considerations of permissibility and prohibition alongside judicial rights, so it is preferable that they be independent in a law of their own. The Ottoman Empire later issued a personal status law in 1336 AH titled 'Qarār Ḥuqūq al-‘Ā'ila', limited to the rulings of marriage and divorce, and introduced therein the system of judicial separation derived from the Maliki madhhab. 3. Criminal punishments, because they were left to the penal code that was issued in 127 AH.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage provides a general overview of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah. It emphasizes that the Majallah was the first official codification of civil law in the modern sense, entirely sourced from Islamic fiqh, specifically Hanafi. Before the Majallah, there was no unified civil code; judges relied on the preponderant Hanafi opinions. The Majallah intentionally excluded three areas: (1) acts of worship (ibādāt), as it was meant for judicial matters, particularly commercial transactions; (2) personal status law, later addressed by a separate Ottoman law (Qarār Ḥuqūq al-‘Ā'ila), which even incorporated elements from the Maliki school for judicial separation; and (3) criminal law, which was covered by a separate penal code. This exclusion highlights the specialized scope of the Majallah and the practical approach of the Ottoman codifiers.

Key Terms
المجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah): The Ottoman civil code, first modern codification of Islamic civil law, based on Hanafi fiqh.
الأحوال الشخصية (al-aḥwāl al-shakhṣiyyah): Personal status law, covering marriage, divorce, inheritance, and related family matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Acts of worship (ibādāt)
B) Personal status (aḥwāl shakhṣiyyah)
C) Commercial transactions (mu‘āmalāt)
D) Criminal punishments (‘uqūbāt)

Key Takeaway
The Majallah's exclusion of worship, personal status, and criminal law reflects a pragmatic approach to codification, focusing on civil transactions while leaving other areas to separate regulation.

The Ottoman Majalla: Codification of Hanafi Juristic Maxims
Describes the Majalla as the first codification of Islamic civil law and its use of legal maxims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the significance of the Ottoman Majalla as a codification of Hanafi fiqh and identify the role and limitations of its 99 general legal maxims in judicial reasoning.

Core Idea
The Majalla codified Hanafi fiqh into 1,851 articles, led by 99 maxims that guide interpretation but cannot stand alone without specific texts.

The Ottoman Majalla was the first official codification of Islamic civil law in the modern sense. It transformed the way Hanafi fiqh was applied in courts by selecting a single authoritative opinion for each issue and organizing rulings into numbered articles. At its forefront, it placed 99 general legal maxims that encapsulate the spirit of the school. However, these maxims are not standalone sources — they require supporting textual authority. This lesson explores the Majalla's structure, its exclusions, and the proper use of its maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose and scope of the Majalla
Determine what the Majalla aimed to achieve and which areas of law it covered.
The Majalla was designed for judicial efficiency, covering civil transactions.
2
List the three areas excluded and explain why
Identify which fiqh subjects were omitted and the reasoning behind each exclusion.
Exclusions: ibadat, personal status, penal law. Each had practical reasons.
3
Examine the role of the 99 general maxims
Understand how the maxims function as overarching principles within the Majalla.
Maxims like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' dominate entire fields of law.
4
Note the caution about using maxims alone
Recognize the committee's warning that a judge cannot rely solely on a maxim without another textual basis.
Every maxim has exceptions that fall under another maxim or specific rule.
5
Evaluate the overall impact of the Majalla
Weigh the advantages and drawbacks of this codification effort as described in the passage.
Advantages include clarity and uniformity; drawbacks include loss of juristic depth.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
وكلما أنهت تحرير كتاب تقدم منه نسخة لمقام مشيخة الإسلام نسخاً أخرى لمن له مهارة ومعرفة كافية في علم الفقه، وتتلقى الملاحظات الواردة عليه وتجري ما يلزم من التهذيب والتعديل في ضوء تلك الملاحظات، ثم تقوم بتبييضه نهائياً وترجمته إلى اللغة العربية وترفعه إلى الصدر الأعظم لتوشيحه بالخط الهمايوني للعمل بموجبه. وآخر كتاب خُتِمَت به المجلة هو كتاب القضاء، وصدرت الإرادة السنية للعمل بالمجلة ما تضمنته في السادس والعشرين من شعبان ١٢٩٣ هـ.
