٣ - أحكام العقوبات، لأنها تركت لقانون الجزاء الذي كان صادراً في ١٢٧ هـ.

ملحق بالفصل (١٦) مجلة الأحكام العدلية
ج - أما القواعد الفقهية الكلية التي وضعت في صدر المجلة، وعددها ٩٩ فقد كانت كل قاعدة منها أو بضع قواعد تسود وتهيمن بحسب موضوعها على مركز فقهي كبير كقاعدة الأمور بمقاصدها التي تضع أساس اختلاف الأحكام القضائية باختلاف حُسن النية وسوئها في الأفعال والتصرفات ؛ وكقاعدة العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني التي تضع أساس رعاية قصد العاقدين في تفسير العقود; وفي أواخرها قواعد تنظيم الإثبات المتعلقة بالإقرار والبينات وحدود سريانهما .
وهذه القواعد لها قيمة كبرى في تكوين المَلَكَة الفقهية، والمدارك والمفاهيم القانونية والقضائية وإن كان تقرير جمعية المجلة قد تضمن تنبيها إلى أنه لا يجوز أن يستند قضاء القاضي إلى شيء من هذه القواعد وحدها دون نص آخر لأن لكل قاعدة في الغالب بعض استثناءات هي أكثر انطباقاً على قاعدة أخرى. 
١٤/١٦ - المجلة في الميزان يعتبر صدور المجلة في حينها حدثاً عظيماً في تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي وحركة التقنين منه. فبها تحقق حلم ابن المقفع منذ عهد الخليفة العباسي أبي جعفر المنصور. ولكنها ككل عمل مبتكر في بدايته يكون له مزايا وعيوب، ويجب أن يخضع للتطوير والتجديد .
ونشير فيما يلي بإيجاز إلى مزايا المجلة وعيوبها :
أ - من مزاياها:
١ - إن المجلة - بالنسبة إلى المدونات الفقهية التي كانت هي مرجع القضاء في الدولة العثمانية قبلها - قد أقامت فارقاً لم يكن من قبل بين أسلوب الفقه الشارح، وأسلوب التشريع الآمر فصاغت من الفقه مرجعاً قضائياً صياغة القوانين الحديثة من حيث الترتيب والترقيم، وطريقة التعبير الآمر وتسهيل العبارة والاقتصار على قول واحد مختار للعمل به في كل مسألة دون الأدلة أو المناقشات والاختلافات التي تزخر بها كتب الفقه،
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. Whenever it finished drafting a book, it submitted a copy to the office of the Shaykh al-Islam, and other copies to those with sufficient skill and knowledge in fiqh. It would receive observations on it and carry out necessary editing and amendment in light of those observations. Then it would finalize it, translate it into Arabic, and submit it to the Grand Vizier for endorsement with the imperial monogram for implementation. The last book concluded in the Majalla is the Book of Judicial Rulings, and the imperial will was issued to implement the Majalla in its entirety on the 26th of Sha'ban 1293 AH. 
All of them in all. 
13/16 - A General Description of the Majalla: a) The Majalla is in fact the first official experiment in civil law in the modern sense of the term 'civil law', taken entirely from Islamic jurisprudence. Before it, there was no general civil law in the Ottoman Empire and its dependent Arab countries such as Syria, Palestine, Iraq, and others. Rather, the judiciary there operated on the soundest and most authoritative opinions of Hanafi jurists, the school of the state. 
b) Three areas of fiqh were excluded from the Majalla: 
1 - Acts of worship, because the purpose of creating the Majalla was to serve the judiciary and new judges and to ease the difficulty of fiqh reference for them in matters not covered by exceptional laws, especially commercial law. 
2 - Personal status matters from marriage to inheritance and everything in between, because they contain many considerations of permissibility and prohibition alongside judicial rights, so it is preferable that they be independent in their own law. The Ottoman Empire later issued a personal status law in 1336 AH titled 'Qarar Huquq al-'A'ila', limited to rulings on marriage and separation, and introduced into it a system of judicial divorce derived from the Maliki school. 
3 - Penal rulings, because they were left to the penal code that had been issued in 127 AH. 

Appendix to Chapter (16): The Majalla of Judicial Rulings 
c) As for the general jurisprudential maxims placed at the beginning of the Majalla, numbering 99, each maxim or a few of them dominate according to their subject over a major fiqh area, such as the maxim 'Matters are according to intentions', which lays the foundation for the difference of judicial rulings according to the difference of good and bad faith in actions and transactions; and the maxim 'Consideration in contracts is for intentions and meanings', which lays the foundation for considering the intention of the contracting parties in interpreting contracts. At the end are maxims regulating evidence related to acknowledgments and proofs and the limits of their validity. 
These maxims have great value in forming juristic acumen and legal and judicial concepts, although the report of the Majalla committee included a caution that the judge's ruling may not rely on any of these maxims alone without another text, because every maxim generally has some exceptions that are more applicable to another maxim. 
14/16 - The Majalla in the Balance. The issuance of the Majalla at its time is considered a great event in the history of Islamic jurisprudence and the movement of codification from it. With it, the dream of Ibn al-Muqaffa' from the era of the Abbasid Caliph Abu Ja'far al-Mansur was realized. But like every innovative work in its beginning, it has advantages and disadvantages, and it must undergo development and renewal. 
We indicate below briefly the advantages and disadvantages of the Majalla: 
A) Among its advantages: 
1 - The Majalla, compared to the fiqh compilations that were the reference for the judiciary in the Ottoman Empire before it, established a difference that did not exist before between the style of explanatory fiqh and the style of imperative legislation. It shaped from fiqh a judicial reference in the mold of modern laws in terms of arrangement, numbering, method of imperative expression, simplification of wording, and limiting to a single chosen opinion for operation in each issue, without the evidences, discussions, and differences that abound in fiqh books.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy description of the Ottoman Majalla (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyyah), the first modern codification of Islamic civil law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, outlines the process of drafting the Majalla, its scope, and its use of 99 general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-fiqhiyyah al-kulliyyah). 

Key points: 
- The Majalla was drafted by a committee that submitted drafts to the Shaykh al-Islam and other scholars for review, finalized, translated into Arabic, and then enacted by imperial decree. 
- It excluded three areas: ibadat (worship), personal status, and penal law. Ibadat were excluded because the Majalla was meant for judicial matters; personal status was later codified separately partly based on Maliki law for judicial divorce; penal law was left to a separate penal code. 
- The 99 maxims placed at the beginning are foundational, such as 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are according to intentions) and 'al-'ibrah fi al-'uqud lil-maqasid wa al-ma'ani' (consideration in contracts is for intentions and meanings). These maxims dominate entire fiqh areas. 
- A critical caution: these maxims cannot be used alone as the exclusive basis for a judicial ruling without another textual authority (nass), because every maxim has exceptions that fall under another maxim. 
- The Majalla is evaluated as a great achievement, realizing a centuries-old dream of clerics like Ibn al-Muqaffa', but it also has drawbacks, the most important being that it introduced a gap between explanatory fiqh and imperative legislation. Its advantages include presenting fiqh in the format of modern civil codes: numbered articles, imperative language, simplified expression, and choosing a single authoritative opinion for each issue without the proliferation of evidence and differences. 

The passage reflects the Hanafi tradition's approach to codification, emphasizing the need for systematic organization of legal rulings while preserving the integrity of the established fiqh.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman civil code (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyyah), a codification of Hanafi fiqh in the form of modern legislation.
القواعد الفقهية الكلية (al-qawa'id al-fiqhiyyah al-kulliyyah): General legal maxims that encapsulate broad principles of Islamic jurisprudence; the Majalla opens with 99 such maxims.
التقنين (al-taqnin): The act of codifying legal rulings into a systematic, authoritative legislative code.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not part of Islamic law.
B) Because the Majalla was intended for judicial matters and judges, and ibadat are not typically adjudicated.
C) Because the committee could not agree on ibadat rulings.
D) Because ibadat were already covered in the penal code.

Key Takeaway
The Majalla codified Hanafi fiqh with 99 guiding maxims, but these maxims require supporting texts and cannot be used independently.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Majallah's Qawa'id: Codification and Critique
Critical evaluation of the Majallah's qawa'id and codification methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the role of qawa'id in the Majallah and identify its advantages and disadvantages as a codification from a Hanafi usul perspective.

Core Idea
Codification transforms fiqh from commentary into law, but general maxims cannot replace specific textual support.

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah was a landmark in Islamic legal history, codifying Hanafi fiqh into a modern legislative code. Central to its structure were 99 comprehensive fiqh maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) intended to guide judicial reasoning. However, as this passage explains, these maxims come with a crucial warning: they cannot be used alone to justify a ruling. In this lesson, we will analyze the role of these maxims, the advantages of codification, and the criticisms leveled against the Majallah's approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the 99 qawa'id
Read the first part of the passage to understand how the maxims were placed at the beginning of the Majallah to govern large fiqh domains. Note specific examples like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and 'al-ibra fi al-uqud li al-maqasid wa al-ma'ani'.
These are not merely introductory; they function as interpretive principles.
2
Recognize the warning against sole reliance
The passage states that a judge cannot base a judgment solely on a qa'ida without another text. Understand why: each maxim has exceptions that are governed by other maxims.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that qawa'id are subsidiary to primary texts.
3
List the advantages of codification
The Majallah shifted from explanatory fiqh (sharh) to imperative legislation. It selected one opinion per issue, simplified language, and eliminated proofs and disagreements.
This made fiqh accessible to non-specialist Ottoman judges.
4
Identify the three disadvantages
The passage mentions: (1) an educational style with definitions and examples, (2) inclusion of procedural law alongside substantive law, and (3) a substantive defect (not elaborated).
The first defect is justified by the practical need of the time.
5
Evaluate the critical refinement
Consider whether the Majallah's approach to codification is suitable for today. The passage implies that codification must evolve. Reflect on the balance between uniformity and juristic discretion.
This is a classic issue in Hanafi legal philosophy.

فأما الأول (عيب الخطة) : فهو أنَّ المجلة اشتملت موادها على إيضاح للمبادىء والأحكام بضرب الأمثلة التطبيقية الطويلة، كما أنت بتعريفات لأمور وأشياء يعتبر تعريفها من قبيل ايضاح الواضحات، كتعريف البائع بأنه هو الذي يبيع وتعريف المشتري بأنه الذي يشتري، وتعريف المتبايعين بأنهما البائع والمشترى. وكل هذا لا ينسجم مع الطريقة التقنينية الحديثة التي تقضي بأن تتسم الصياغة القانونية بطابع الأمر، باعتبار أن القانون آمر وليس بمعلم. فمدار الإيضاح التطبيقي والتعليمي إنما هو : الشروح وليس متن القانون. ولكن لهذا العيب تغطية هي أن المجلة وضعت لسد حاجة موظفين وقضاة نظاميين معظمهم من الأتراك وليس لهم دراسة شرعية، فحاجة البيئة إذ ذاك تستدعي هذا الصنيع الإيضاحي. ٢ - Appendix to Chapter (16) of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah: C) As for the comprehensive fiqh maxims that were placed at the beginning of the Majallah, numbering 99, each maxim or a few maxims dominated and governed, according to their subject, a large fiqh domain, such as the maxim 'Matters are judged by their purposes/objects' which lays the basis for the difference of judicial rulings based on good or bad intention in actions and transactions; and the maxim 'Consideration in contracts is given to purposes and meanings' which lays the basis for observing the contracting parties' intent in interpreting contracts; and at their end are rules regulating evidence related to confession and proofs and the limits of their application. These maxims have great value in developing juristic skill, legal and judicial understanding and concepts, although the Majallah committee's report included a warning that a judge's judgment should not be based on any of these maxims alone without another text, because each maxim generally has some exceptions that are more in line with another maxim. 14/16) The Majallah in the Balance: The issuance of the Majallah in its time is considered a great event in the history of Islamic fiqh and the movement of codification from it. Through it, the dream of Ibn al-Muqaffa' since the era of the Abbasid Caliph Abu Ja'far al-Mansur was realized. However, like every innovative work in its beginning, it has advantages and disadvantages, and it must undergo development and renewal. We briefly indicate below the advantages and disadvantages of the Majallah: A) Among its advantages: 1) The Majallah - in comparison to the fiqh compilations that were the reference of the judiciary in the Ottoman Empire before it - established a distinction that did not exist before between the style of explanatory fiqh and the style of imperative legislation. It formulated from fiqh a judicial reference in the formula of modern laws in terms of arrangement, numbering, method of imperative expression, simplification of phrasing, and limiting to one chosen opinion to be acted upon in each issue, without the proofs, discussions, and differences that abound in fiqh books, and whose field is explanatory fiqh, not the judicial reference, although these are a great legal and intellectual wealth that enlightens the mind of the student and legal researcher. Its disadvantages: The Majallah has three disadvantages by the standards of modern codification theory. One of those disadvantages relates to the legal expression plan, the second to the formal aspect, and the third to the substantive aspect. 1) As for the first (plan defect): The Majallah included in its articles explanations of principles and rulings by giving long practical examples, and it gave definitions of matters and things whose definition is considered a clarification of obvious matters, such as defining the seller as the one who sells, and the buyer as the one who buys, and the two contracting parties as the seller and the buyer. All this is inconsistent with the modern codification method which requires that legal drafting be characterized by an imperative tone, given that law commands and does not teach. The sphere of practical and educational explanation is in commentaries, not in the text of the law. However, this defect has a justification: the Majallah was drafted to meet the need of regular employees and judges, most of whom were Turks and had no Sharia studies; the need of the environment at that time necessitated this explanatory practice. 2) As for the defect related to the formal aspect: The Majallah included provisions of the section that is today called procedural law or procedure or formal provisions, while civil law in its modern concept only contains substantive provisions, not formal ones. The reason for that is that in fiqh books, formal provisions were recorded alongside substantive provisions in overlapping chapters, and the Majallah followed the fiqh sources from which it derived in that. This does not affect the essence of codification but is a matter of preference.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Hanafi-based civil code of the Ottoman Empire. The author first discusses the role of the 99 comprehensive fiqh maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) that preface the Majallah. These maxims, such as 'Matters are judged by their objects' (al-umur bi-maqasidiha) and 'Consideration in contracts is given to purposes and meanings' (al-ibra fi al-uqud li al-maqasid wa al-ma'ani), served as overarching principles guiding judicial reasoning. However, the author notes an important warning: judges cannot rely solely on these maxims without specific textual support, because each maxim has exceptions governed by other maxims. The passage then moves to a balanced assessment of the Majallah's merits and demerits. Its primary advantage was that it transformed the style of fiqh from explanatory commentary into an imperative legislative code, selecting one authoritative opinion per issue and eliminating the multiplicity of views typical of fiqh books. This made it accessible for non-specialist judges. The three disadvantages are: (1) an educational, example-laden style inappropriate for modern legislation; (2) inclusion of procedural law (usul al-muhakamat) alongside substantive law, contrary to modern civil codes; and (3) a third defect (not fully elaborated in the text) related to substantive content. The author justifies the first defect by noting the pragmatic need to train Ottoman judges lacking Sharia training. This analysis is part of the broader philosophy of Islamic legal codification (taqnin), which the Majallah pioneered.

Key Terms
القواعد الفقهية الكلية (al-qawāʿid al-fiqhiyya al-kulliyya): The comprehensive fiqh maxims, numbering 99, placed at the beginning of the Majallah to govern large areas of fiqh, such as the maxim 'Matters are judged by their objects'.
التقنين (al-taqnīn): The process of codifying fiqh into a modern legislative code, as exemplified by the Majallah, which transformed explanatory fiqh into imperative statutory provisions.
الاستثناء (al-istithnāʾ): Exception to a qa'ida, where another qa'ida supersedes it. The passage warns that each qa'ida generally has exceptions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It included both substantive and procedural law in a single code.

Key Takeaway
The Majallah's use of qawa'id as guiding principles is both a strength and a limitation; they must be applied with specific textual support.

Three Defects of the Majalla's Codification
Critical analysis of codification methodology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the three defects of the Majalla's codification methodology as critiqued by Mustafa al-Zarqa, and apply his critique to assess modern codifications of Islamic law.

Core Idea
A single-madhhab code cannot meet evolving societal needs; the Majalla's three defects show the importance of flexible, multi-madhhab codification.

Mustafa al-Zarqa, a 20th-century Syrian jurist, critically examined the Ottoman Majalla, the first codification of Hanafi civil law. While acknowledging its historical value, he identified three major defects: an overly didactic plan, mixing procedural with substantive law, and exclusive adherence to the Hanafi school. This lesson unpacks each defect and its implications for legal codification, drawing lessons for modern fatwa and law-making.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three defects
Read the passage and list the three defects al-Zarqa attributes to the Majalla. Note the order: plan, formal, substantive.
The third defect (substantive) is the most important.
2
Analyze the defect of plan
Understand why including explanatory examples and definitions is problematic in modern codification. Consider the mitigating factor al-Zarqa mentions: the need to train non-specialist judges.
Modern codes are imperative, not didactic.
3
Analyze the formal defect
Examine why mixing substantive and procedural law is considered a flaw. Recognize that this was inherited from fiqh books and is a matter of legislative taste.
It does not affect the essence of codification.
4
Analyze the substantive defect
Focus on al-Zarqa's key criticism: exclusive reliance on one madhhab. Evaluate why he argues that no single school can suffice for all legislative needs.
This defect led to the Majalla's inadequacy for modern economic transactions.
5
Evaluate the critique's relevance
Consider the consequences of the Majalla's defects: successive amendments and repeals. Reflect on how this critique informs modern codification efforts in Muslim jurisdictions.
The lesson applies to any attempt to codify Islamic law using a single source.

فهي من قبيل الذوق العصري في التصنيف القانوني. وقد يأتي يوم يرجع هذا الذوق إلى القديم فيرى جمع القسمين في قانون واحد.
وهذا كما نرى أن قوانين الشكل نفسها إلى اليوم تشتمل على أصول المرافعات وأصول التنفيذ بينما يتَّجه الذوق القانوني اليوم إلى فصل أصول التنفيذ في قانون مستقل .
٣ ـ وأما العيب العائد إلى الناحية الموضوعية) فإنه الأمر المهم، وهو أنَّ المجلة قد التزمت مذهباً واحداً هو المذهب الحنفي.
ولا مراء أن المذهب الاجتهادي الواحد مهما اتسع بأصوله وفروعه وتشعبت نظرياته ،وتخريجاته لا يمكن أن يكفي الأمة في حاجاتها التشريعية المتجدّدة. فالسَّعة الكبرى في قابليات الفقه الإسلامي العظيم إنما تتجلى في مجموع مذاهبه الاجتهادية لا في واحد منها فكان الواجب أن تستمد المجلة من جميع المذاهب الفقهية أحسن ما في كل منها وأعدله وأجراه مع المصلحة الزمنية وحاجات المجتمع المقبل على تطور كبير في مجالات الاقتصاد والتعامل نتيجة الاتصال الاقتصادي والسياسي والثقافي بين الشرق والغرب الجديد .
ولذا لم يمض على صدور المجلة زمن طويل حتى ظهر عدم كفايتها لوفاء الحاجات العصرية التي نشأت من تولد الأساليب الاقتصادية الحديثة في التجارة وفي العمل وسائر نواحي الإنتاج، مما دعا إلى تدارك الحاجة بقوانين متتابعة عديدة كان كل منها ينسخ جانباً من المجلة .
وكان من أبرز ما نُسخ منها أحكام المشارطات العقدية، فإن نطاقها في المذهب الحنفي ضيق والحاجة إليها أصبحت ملحة، فلجأت الدولة إلى تعديل أحكام بطلان العقود وفسادها والشروط المفسدة، وأطلقت حرية المشارطات العقدية سوى ما يخل منها بالآداب والنظام العام وبعض
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. ... Its shortcomings: The Majalla has three defects by the standards of modern codification theory. One of those defects relates to the legal exposition plan, the second to the formal aspect, and the third to the substantive aspect. 1- As for the first (defect of plan): that the Majalla's articles included explanation of principles and rulings by giving long applied examples, and it gave definitions of things and matters that are considered clarifying the obvious, such as defining the seller as he who sells, and the buyer as he who buys, and defining the two contracting parties as the seller and the buyer. All of this is inconsistent with the modern codification method which requires that legal drafting bear an imperative character, considering that the law commands and does not teach. For applied and educational explanation is the domain of commentaries, not the text of the law. But there is a mitigating factor for this defect: that the Majalla was designed to meet the needs of regular employees and judges, most of whom were Turks with no religious law training, so the need of the environment at that time justified this explanatory approach. 2- As for the defect relating to the formal aspect: that the Majalla included rulings of the section today called principles of litigation or procedure or formal rulings, whereas the civil law in its modern concept contains only substantive rulings without procedural ones. The reason is that fiqh books recorded procedural rulings alongside substantive rulings in overlapping chapters, and the Majalla followed the fiqh sources from which it derived. This does not affect the essence of codification; it is a matter of taste. ... 3- As for the defect relating to the substantive aspect: it is the important matter, namely that the Majalla adhered to a single madhhab, the Hanafi madhhab. Undoubtedly, a single ijtihadi madhhab, no matter how extensive in its principles and branches and how diverse its theories and derivations, cannot suffice for the nation in its renewed legislative needs. The great capacity of the magnificent Islamic fiqh is manifested only in the totality of its ijtihadi madhhabs, not in just one of them. So the obligation was that the Majalla should have drawn from all fiqh madhhabs the best and most just of each and most in line with temporal interest and the needs of a society facing great development in economics and transactions due to economic, political, and cultural contact between East and the new West. Therefore, not long after the Majalla's enactment, its insufficiency to meet modern needs became apparent, arising from new modern economic methods in trade, labor, and other aspects of production, necessitating successive laws that each repealed part of the Majalla. Among the most prominent things repealed were the rulings on contractual stipulations, because their scope in the Hanafi madhhab was narrow while the need for them became urgent, so the state resorted to amending the rulings on contract invalidity and corruption and vitiating conditions, and granted freedom of contractual stipulations except those that violate morals, public order, and some.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents a critical evaluation of the Ottoman Majalla (مجلة الأحكام العدلية) by Mustafa al-Zarqa, a 20th-century Syrian jurist. The author identifies three defects of the Majalla when measured by modern codification standards. First, the defect of plan: the Majalla included explanatory definitions and long applied examples, which is contrary to the modern style where a civil code should be imperative and concise, leaving explanations to commentaries. However, al-Zarqa notes this was a necessary accommodation for non-specialist judges and officials. Second, the formal defect: the Majalla mixed substantive civil law with procedural law (principles of litigation). This was inherited from classical fiqh works that intermingled both. Al-Zarqa considers this a matter of taste, not a fundamental flaw, as procedural codes today sometimes also combine topics. Third, the substantive defect (most important): the Majalla committed itself exclusively to the Hanafi madhhab. Al-Zarqa argues that no single madhhab can meet all the evolving needs of the Muslim community; the richness of Islamic law lies in its multiple schools. He points out that soon after its enactment, the Majalla proved inadequate for modern economic developments, especially in the area of contractual stipulations (المشارطات العقدية), which the Hanafi school treated restrictively. Consequently, the Ottoman state had to amend or repeal parts of the Majalla, notably liberalizing contractual freedom. This passage is a legal philosophy analysis of codification methodology, critiquing the limitations of a single-madhhab code.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Civil Code (Majalla al-Ahkam al-Adaliyya), a codification of Hanafi fiqh in civil transactions, enacted in the 19th century.
المذهب الحنفي (al-madhhab al-Hanafi): The Hanafi school of Islamic law, the official madhhab of the Ottoman Empire, exclusively adopted in the Majalla.
المشارطات العقدية (al-musharatat al-'aqdiyya): Contractual stipulations or conditions agreed upon by parties in a contract. The Hanafi madhhab had a narrow view on permissible stipulations.
العيب الموضوعي (al-'ayb al-mawdu'i): The substantive defect: exclusive adherence to one madhhab, considered the most important defect by al-Zarqa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Mustafa al-Zarqa, which defect of the Majalla was the most important?
A) The defect of plan – including explanatory examples and definitions
B) The formal defect – mixing substantive and procedural law
C) The substantive defect – adhering exclusively to the Hanafi madhhab
D) The defect of language – being written in Ottoman Turkish

Key Takeaway
The Majalla's greatest flaw was its exclusive reliance on the Hanafi madhhab, which prevented it from adapting to modern economic and social changes.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Critique of Single-Madhhab Codification: The Case of the Ottoman Majalla
Critique of the Majalla's exclusive Hanafi madhhab approach with examples from contract law, property, and procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the methodological limitation of restricting codification to a single madhhab and identify alternative positions from other schools that could better meet legislative needs.

Core Idea
A single madhhab cannot meet all evolving legal needs – Islamic law's strength lies in its collective diversity.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa’s work critiques the Ottoman Majalla for confining itself to the Hanafi madhhab. Al-Zarqa argues that no single ijtihadi school, however comprehensive, can suffice for the ummah's changing circumstances. He provides concrete examples where the Majalla's Hanafi-centric rulings proved inadequate, necessitating later amendments and suggesting alternative rulings from other madhhabs. This lesson examines his critique, the rationale behind it, and its implications for the practice of ifta and legal codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Critique
State the primary substantive defect al-Zarqa identifies in the Majalla: its exclusive adherence to the Hanafi madhhab. Note that he considers this a major weakness.
The defect is 'al-ʿayb al-ʿāʾid ilā al-nāḥiya al-mawḍūʿiyya' – the substantive side defect.
2
Understand the Rationale
Explain why al-Zarqa believes a single madhhab is insufficient. He argues that the capacity of Islamic fiqh is manifested in the sum of its madhhabs, not in one alone. The Majalla should have drawn from all schools to meet evolving needs.
Key phrase: 'al-saʿa al-kubrā fī qābiliyyāt al-fiqh al-islāmī... fī majmūʿ madhāhibihi al-ijtihādiyya.'
3
Examine the First Example: Contractual Stipulations
The Hanafi school is restrictive on conditions in contracts. The Majalla's rules on mushāriṭāt ʿaqdiyya were narrow, leading to urgent need for liberalization. The state amended by inserting Article 64 into the Procedural Law. Al-Zarqa suggests adopting the positions of Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, or the Hanbali school.
These alternative views align with the principle 'al-ʿaqd shariʿat al-mutaʿāqidayn.'
4
Explore Other Examples
List additional areas where the Majalla should have used other schools: (1) māliyyat al-manāfiʿ – adopt Shafi'i/Hanbali view that usufructs are guaranteed property; (2) shufʿa – adopt Maliki rules on time limits and price disputes; (3) radd al-yamīn ʿalā al-muddaʿī and ʿadam tajazzuʾ al-iqrār – adopt schools that permit these procedures.
The common thread is adopting the more flexible or practically suitable ruling from another madhhab.
5
Synthesize the Broader Principle
Al-Zarqa advocates for takhayyur (eclectic selection) across madhhabs when codifying law or issuing fatwas. This ensures the law remains adaptable and serves the maqāṣid and temporal needs. The mufti or legislator should not be rigidly bound to one madhhab.
This is a foundational principle in uṣūl al-iftā: considering maslaha and context, and not confining oneself to a single school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو جمع بين ملحق بالفصل (١٦) مجلة الأحكام العدلية أجزاء القانون لا تفترق النتائج العملية بين فصلها أو جمعها. فهي من قبيل الذوق العصري في التصنيف القانوني. وقد يأتي يوم يرجع هذا الذوق إلى القديم فيرى جمع القسمين في قانون واحد. وهذا كما نرى أن قوانين الشكل نفسها إلى اليوم تشتمل على أصول المرافعات وأصول التنفيذ بينما يتَّجه الذوق القانوني اليوم إلى فصل أصول التنفيذ في قانون مستقل .
٣ ـ وأما العيب العائد إلى الناحية الموضوعية) فإنه الأمر المهم، وهو أنَّ المجلة قد التزمت مذهباً واحداً هو المذهب الحنفي. ولا مراء أن المذهب الاجتهادي الواحد مهما اتسع بأصوله وفروعه وتشعبت نظرياته ،وتخريجاته لا يمكن أن يكفي الأمة في حاجاتها التشريعية المتجدّدة. فالسَّعة الكبرى في قابليات الفقه الإسلامي العظيم إنما تتجلى في مجموع مذاهبه الاجتهادية لا في واحد منها فكان الواجب أن تستمد المجلة من جميع المذاهب الفقهية أحسن ما في كل منها وأعدله وأجراه مع المصلحة الزمنية وحاجات المجتمع المقبل على تطور كبير في مجالات الاقتصاد والتعامل نتيجة الاتصال الاقتصادي والسياسي والثقافي بين الشرق والغرب الجديد .
ولذا لم يمض على صدور المجلة زمن طويل حتى ظهر عدم كفايتها لوفاء الحاجات العصرية التي نشأت من تولد الأساليب الاقتصادية الحديثة في التجارة وفي العمل وسائر نواحي الإنتاج، مما دعا إلى تدارك الحاجة بقوانين متتابعة عديدة كان كل منها ينسخ جانباً من المجلة .
وكان من أبرز ما نُسخ منها أحكام المشارطات العقدية، فإن نطاقها في المذهب الحنفي ضيق والحاجة إليها أصبحت ملحة، فلجأت الدولة إلى تعديل أحكام بطلان العقود وفسادها والشروط المفسدة، وأطلقت حرية المشارطات العقدية سوى ما يخل منها بالآداب والنظام العام وبعض مستثنيات أخرى، وذلك بمادة قانونية أزلقتها إزلاقاً في قانون أصول المحاكمات الحقوقية في المادة / ٦٤ منه .
ولو أن جمعية المجلة لم تقصرها على المذهب الحنفي، بل استمدت فيها من كل المذاهب الاجتهادية ما تراه أصلح وأوفى بالحاجة الزمنية لما احتيج بعد صدورها بمدة قصيرة إلى إعمال المباضع الجراحية في جسمها وما غادرته فيها من تعطيل أعضاء وتفريغ أحشاء مما أدخل عليها من نسخ ومسخ وتعديل .
ففي مجموع المذاهب الفقهية متسع لوفاء الحاجات الزمنية، وكان الاستمداد منها يجعل المجلة أكثر ثباتاً واستقراراً على الزمن . فقد كان الواجب مثلاً في أحكام المشارطات العقدية - وهي أهم ما ظهر فيه ضيق المجلة - أن تستمد من مذهب شُرَيح القاضي وابن شبرمة أو المذهب الحنبلي، فإنّ مذاهب هؤلاء في صحة الشروط التعاقدية وبطلانها يجاري أحسن ما استقرت عليه النظريات القانونية الحديثة في ضابط ما يقبل وما يرفض من المشارطات في ضوء المبدأ القائل: إنَّ العقد شريعة المتعاقدين.
وكان من الواجب مثلاً أيضاً في قضية مالية المنافع، وتقومها في ذاتها، وضمان منافع المغصوب أن تستمد أحكامها من المذهبين الشافعي والحنبلي اللذين يعتبران المنافع أموالاً مضمونة بذاتها كالأعيان. وكان الواجب في طلب الشفعة أن تستمد أحكام المدد المسقطة للشفعة والاختلاف في ثمن العقار المشفوع من المذهب المالكي. وكذا تستمد في قضية الشاهد واليمين وفي رد اليمين على المدعي، وفي عدم تجزؤ الإقرار من المذاهب الاجتهادية التي تقول بذلك لظهور الحاجة إلى كل ذلك مما هو غير مقبول في حكم المجلة المستمد من المذهب الحنفي.
وهكذا يقال في مواطن وقضايا ومسائل أخرى مما لا يتسع المجال لتعداده كمسألة البيع بما سيستقر عليه السعر في المذهب الحنبلي.
Or a combination. Appendix to Section (16) of the Majalla: Parts of the law do not differ in practical results whether separated or combined. So it is a matter of modern taste in legal codification. A day may come when this taste reverts to the old, seeing the combination of both sections in one law. This is as we see that the procedural laws themselves to this day include principles of litigation and principles of execution, while legal taste today tends to separate the principles of execution into an independent law.
3- As for the defect related to the substantive side, it is the important matter: that the Majalla adhered to a single madhhab, namely the Hanafi madhhab. There is no doubt that a single ijtihadi madhhab, no matter how broad its principles and branches and how intricate its theories and derivations, cannot suffice the ummah in its renewed legislative needs. The great capacity of the magnificent Islamic fiqh is only manifested in the sum of its ijtihadi madhhabs, not in any one of them. So it was necessary for the Majalla to draw from all fiqh madhhabs the best of each, the most just, and the most in line with temporal interest and the needs of a society facing great development in economics and transactions as a result of economic, political, and cultural contact between East and the new West.
Therefore, not long after the Majalla was issued, its insufficiency to meet contemporary needs became apparent—needs arising from the emergence of modern economic methods in trade, labor, and all other aspects of production. This necessitated addressing the need through numerous successive laws, each of which repealed a part of the Majalla.
Among the most notable of what was repealed were the rulings on contractual stipulations (musharitat). Their scope in the Hanafi madhhab is narrow, yet the need for them became urgent. So the state resorted to amending the rulings on void and corrupt contracts and invalidating conditions, and it liberated the freedom of contractual stipulations except those that violate morals, public order, and some other exceptions. This was done by a legal provision that she slipped in (azlaqatha izlaaqan) into the Procedural Law (Qanun Usul al-Muhakamat al-Huquqiyya), Article 64.
Had the Majalla commission not confined it to the Hanafi madhhab, but rather derived from all ijtihadi madhhabs what it deemed more suitable and more fulfilling of temporal needs, there would have been no need shortly after its issuance to apply surgical scalpels to its body, leaving it with disabled organs and emptied entrails through the repeal, distortion, and amendment that were inflicted upon it.
Indeed, in the sum of the fiqh madhhabs there is ample scope to fulfill temporal needs, and deriving from them would have made the Majalla more stable and enduring over time. For example, in the rulings on contractual stipulations—which are the most important area where the Majalla's narrowness appeared—it should have derived from the madhhab of Shurayh al-Qadi and Ibn Shubruma or the Hanbali madhhab. Their rulings on the validity and invalidity of contractual conditions align with the best modern legal theories regarding the criterion for accepting or rejecting stipulations in light of the principle: 'The contract is the law of the contracting parties.'
Similarly, in the issue of the monetary nature of usufructs (manafi'), their valuation in themselves, and the guarantee of the usufruct of a usurped item, the Majalla should have derived its rulings from the Shafi'i and Hanbali madhhabs, which consider usufructs as wealth guaranteed in themselves like corporeal assets. In the matter of pre-emption (shuf'a), it should have derived the rulings on the periods that cancel pre-emption and the disagreement on the price of the pre-empted property from the Maliki madhhab. Likewise, in the matter of the witness and the oath, the refusal of the oath to the plaintiff, and the indivisibility of the acknowledgment (iqrar), it should have derived from the ijtihadi madhhabs that adopt these positions, due to the manifest need for all of this—things not accepted in the ruling of the Majalla derived from the Hanafi madhhab.
And so it is said in other places, issues, and cases too numerous to enumerate, such as the issue of selling at the price that will later be settled (al-bay' bima sayastaqirru 'alayhi al-si'r) in the Hanbali madhhab.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a critical legal philosophy analysis of the Ottoman Majalla (مجلة الأحكام العدلية), specifically addressing its methodological flaw of restricting itself to a single madhhab (the Hanafi school). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, argues that no single ijtihadi madhhab can comprehensively meet the evolving legislative needs of the Muslim community. He explains that the true breadth and potential of Islamic fiqh lie in the collective body of all its schools, not in any one of them. Therefore, the Majalla should have drawn from all madhhabs, selecting the best and most suitable rulings for each context.

Al-Zarqa provides several concrete examples where the Majalla's Hanafi-centric approach proved inadequate, leading to subsequent amendments and repeals. The most prominent example is contractual stipulations (المشارطات العقدية): the Hanafi school is restrictive regarding conditions in contracts, but modern commercial needs demanded greater freedom. The state had to amend the law by surreptitiously inserting an article into the procedural law to allow more liberal stipulation. Al-Zarqa suggests that instead, the Majalla should have adopted the more flexible positions of early Hanafi judges like Shurayh and Ibn Shubruma, or the Hanbali school, which aligns better with modern contract law principles (the contract is the law of the contracting parties).

Other examples include: (1) treating usufructs (manafi') as valuable property guaranteed in themselves, following the Shafi'i or Hanbali view rather than the Hanafi position; (2) using Maliki rulings on pre-emption periods and price disputes; (3) adopting procedures like the refusal of the oath to the plaintiff and indivisibility of acknowledgment from schools that permit them.

The passage reflects a broader legal philosophy that Islamic legislation must be dynamic and adaptable, and that the mufti or legislator should not be rigidly bound to one madhhab when another offers a more suitable solution. This is a foundational critique relevant to usul al-iftaa and the methodology of issuing fatwas, emphasizing the concept of takhayyur (eclectic selection) and the need for the mufti to consider the maqasid (higher objectives) and temporal context.

Key Terms
المذهب الاجتهادي الواحد (al-madhhab al-ijtihādi al-wāḥid): A single school of juristic interpretation, here specifically referring to the Hanafi madhhab as the sole source of the Majalla.
المشارطات العقدية (al-mushāriṭāt al-ʿaqdiyya): Contractual stipulations or conditions imposed by the parties in a contract.
مالية المنافع (māliyyat al-manāfiʿ): The property/wealth nature of usufructs; whether usufructs are considered valuable assets in themselves.
الشفعة (al-shufʿa): Pre-emption, the right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy a sold share before a stranger.
رد اليمين على المدعي (radd al-yamīn ʿalā al-muddaʿī): The procedure of returning or referring the oath to the plaintiff when the defendant refuses to swear.
عدم تجزؤ الإقرار (ʿadam tajazzuʾ al-iqrār): The indivisibility of an acknowledgment; an admission must be accepted or rejected as a whole, not partially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what was the primary substantive defect of the Ottoman Majalla?
A) It did not include procedural rules.
B) It exclusively adhered to the Hanafi madhhab.
C) It was not based on any madhhab.
D) It only used the Maliki madhhab.

Key Takeaway
The capacity of Islamic fiqh to meet evolving needs is realized through the collective body of its schools, not by confining to one; codification and ifta should practice takhayyur across madhhabs.

From Individual Ijtihad to Collective Ijtihad: The Modern Fiqh Academy Movement
Historical argument for collective ijtihad and fiqh academies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for collective ijtihad in modern times and identify the key methodological requirements for a contemporary Islamic civil code.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad through fiqh academies revives the spirit of ijtihad while avoiding the perils of individual error.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work describes the modern movement to revitalize Islamic jurisprudence by establishing institutions for collective ijtihad. It outlines the historical context of attempts to codify Islamic law and argues that only through consultation among qualified scholars can jurisprudence meet contemporary needs without the dangers that led to the closure of individual ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical problem
Note the earlier efforts to create a modern Islamic civil code and the obstacles faced, including the closure of individual ijtihad.
The passage mentions the Syrian attempt in 1949 and its cancellation.
2
Understand the two requirements
The author states that a modern Islamic code must draw from all recognized schools and use new deduction (qiyas, istihsan, istislah).
This is a shift from the Hanafi-only Majalla.
3
Recognize the shift to collective ijtihad
The remedy for the drawbacks of individual ijtihad is to transfer it to a collective body of trustworthy jurists practicing shura.
This eliminates the fears that caused the door of ijtihad to be closed.
4
Trace the practical outcome
The idea leads to the establishment of fiqh academies and conferences, as listed at the end of the passage.
Examples include the Islamic Jurisprudence Week in Damascus and the Riyadh conference.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
مصدر التشريع وفي بعض آخر نصوص على أنَّ الفقه الإسلامي هو مصدر رئيسي، أو هو المصدر الرئيسي للتشريع.
وفي إثر ذلك بدأت المطالبات بوضع قانون مدني حديث مستمد الفقه الإسلامي. وبدأت اهتمامات على صعيد الأفراد من فقهاء الشريعة والقانون بوضع نماذج تنفيذية لذلك، وتلاها بعض اهتمامات على الصعيد الرسمي: فمثلاً، أوفدت وزارة العدل السورية سنة / ١٩٤٩/م أحد كبار القضاة إلى مصر، وزوّدته بكتاب إلى الدكتور عبد الرزاق السنهوري القانوني الشهير (واضع القانون المدني المصري الجديد والذي اتجه إلى دراسة وتدريس الفقه الإسلامي ليتعاونا في وضع قانون مدني مستمد من الفقه الإسلامي.
ثم صادف أن وقع في العام نفسه أول انقلاب عسكري في سورية أطاح بالحكم الدستوري واختلت فيه الموازين، وألغي مشروع القانون المدني المستمد من الفقه الإسلامي، وأخذ بدلاً منه القانون المصري الأجنبي الأصول. لكن تيار المطالبة بالرجوع إلى الأصالة والسعي الدؤوب لتحقيق ذلك المشروع ظل قوياً في سورية ومصر وعدد من البلاد العربية والإسلامية. وقد تمخض هذا الاتجاه العصري - نحو تجديد الفقه والاستمداد منه عن نتائج ونشاطات عظيمة الأهمية سنوجزها فيما يلي:
۲/۱۷ - الدعوة إلى الاجتهاد الجماعي، ونشوء المجامع الفقهية : كانت فكرة قانون مدني إسلامي حديث مستجيب لحاجات العصر الجديد تتطلب أمرين لا بد منهما :
- الاستمداد من الفقه الإسلامي بمفهومه العام، أي بجميع مذاهبه ومدارسه المعتبرة دون الاقتصار على مذهب واحد كما صيغت المجلة في أواخر العهد العثماني من المذهب الحنفي فقط .
- التخريج الجديد على أصول الفقه ومقاصد الشريعة، واللجوء إلى
استخدام القياس والاستحسان والاستصلاح فيما سكتت عنه المذاهب الفقهية في نصوصها القديمة من قضايا الساعة والأمور المستجدة، كعقد التأمين وشركات المساهمة (الأنونيم) وسواهما.
وهذا يستلزم ممارسة الاجتهاد، كما فعل فقهاء السلف فيما واجهوا من المستجدات. فلا بد أن ينفخ الروح في الاجتهاد من جديد بعد أن أقفل بابه منذ القرن الخامس الهجري.
وإذا كانت المخاوف السابقة إذ ذاك من الاجتهاد الذي كان يمارسه الأفراد قد أوجبت ذلك التدبير الوقائي بإقفال بابه، كما سبق بيانه، فإن العلاج في نقله من عهدة الأفراد إلى عاتق الجماعة، فيصبح الاجتهاد جماعياً يمارسه فقهاء العصر الثقات بطريق الشورى فيما بينهم بعد أن كان فردياً يمارسه كل فقيه بمفرده فبذلك تتحقق ثمرات الاجتهاد، إذ لا يمكن أن يلبي الفقه حاجات العصور دونه، وتنتفي بذلك محاذير الاجتهاد الفردي التي دعت إلى إقفال بابه .
ومن هنا نبتت فكرة إنشاء مجمع فقهي يضم نخبة من فقهاء العصر مختلف البلاد الإسلامية يكون له مكتب دائم ودورات اجتماع سنوية تقدم فيها البحوث وتناقش قضايا الساعة وتُقَرّر فيها الحلول المناسبة في ضوء أصول الشريعة ومقاصدها العامة، وآراء الفقهاء السابقين وحاجة العصر . وقد كان من ثمرة هذه الدعوة والاتجاه انعقاد مؤتمرات للفقه الإسلامي نذكر منها.
- أسبوع الفقه الإسلامي بدمشق في شوال ۱۳۸۰هـ (= نيسان ١٩٦١م).
ب - مؤتمر مجمع البحوث الإسلامية بالأزهر في القاهرة، ١٩٦١م.
ج - مؤتمر مدينة البيضاء في ليبيا الذي دعت إليه الجامعة الليبية .
- مؤتمر الفقه الإسلامي في الرياض عام ١٣٩٦هـ (= ١٩٧٦م) بدعوة من
Chapter Three: Periods of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
Source of legislation, and in some other texts it is stated that Islamic jurisprudence is a main source, or it is the main source of legislation.
Following that, demands began to formulate a modern civil code derived from Islamic jurisprudence. Interests began at the individual level from jurists of Sharia and law to formulate executive models for that, followed by some official interests: for example, the Syrian Ministry of Justice in 1949 sent a senior judge to Egypt, providing him with a letter to Dr. Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri, the famous jurist (draftsman of the new Egyptian civil code who turned to studying and teaching Islamic jurisprudence) to cooperate in drafting a civil code derived from Islamic jurisprudence.
Then it happened that in the same year the first military coup in Syria occurred, overthrowing constitutional government, disrupting balances, and the project of a civil code derived from Islamic jurisprudence was cancelled, and instead the Egyptian law of foreign origin was adopted. But the tide of demand for returning to authenticity and the persistent effort to achieve that project remained strong in Syria, Egypt, and a number of Arab and Islamic countries. This modern trend—towards renewing jurisprudence and deriving from it—produced results and activities of great importance, which we will summarize as follows:
2/17 - The call for collective ijtihad, and the emergence of fiqh academies: The idea of a modern Islamic civil code responsive to the needs of the new age required two indispensable matters:
- Derivation from Islamic jurisprudence in its general concept, i.e., from all its recognized schools and doctrines, not limited to one school as the Majalla was drafted in the late Ottoman era from the Hanafi school only.
- New deduction based on usul al-fiqh and maqasid al-shariah, and resorting to the use of qiyas, istihsan, and istislah regarding contemporary issues and new matters that the jurisprudential schools did not address in their classical texts, such as insurance contracts, joint-stock companies (anonymous), and others.
And this necessitates practicing ijtihad, as the salaf jurists did regarding the novelties they faced. So the spirit must be breathed into ijtihad anew after its door was closed since the fifth century AH.
And if the previous fears at that time of ijtihad practiced by individuals necessitated that precautionary measure of closing its door, as explained earlier, then the remedy lies in transferring it from the responsibility of individuals to the collective, so that ijtihad becomes collective, practiced by trustworthy contemporary jurists through mutual consultation, after it was individual, practiced by each jurist alone. Thus the fruits of ijtihad are achieved, for jurisprudence cannot meet the needs of the ages without it, and the drawbacks of individual ijtihad that led to closing its door are eliminated.
Hence sprouted the idea of establishing a fiqh academy comprising an elite of contemporary jurists from various Islamic countries, with a permanent office and annual sessions where research is presented, contemporary issues are discussed, and appropriate solutions are decided in light of the principles of Sharia, its general objectives, the opinions of earlier jurists, and the need of the age. Among the fruits of this call and trend were the convening of Islamic jurisprudence conferences, of which we mention:
- Islamic Jurisprudence Week in Damascus, Shawwal 1380 AH (= April 1961 CE).
B - Conference of the Islamic Research Academy at al-Azhar in Cairo, 1961 CE.
C - Conference of the city of Al-Bayda in Libya, called by the Libyan University.
- Islamic Jurisprudence Conference in Riyadh in 1396 AH (= 1976 CE) called by
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the eighth period of Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the modern revival movement. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, describes how after initial efforts to create a modern civil code derived from Islamic jurisprudence (like the attempt in Syria in 1949), there was a shift towards collective ijtihad. The key idea is that to address contemporary issues, it is necessary to draw from all Islamic schools of thought, not just one, and to use methods like qiyas, istihsan, and istislah for new issues. The passage argues that ijtihad should be performed collectively by trusted scholars through consultation, to avoid the drawbacks of individual ijtihad that led to its closure. This led to the establishment of fiqh academies and conferences. This is a legal philosophy discussion about the methodology of fiqh development.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihad al-jama'i): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of qualified jurists through consultation (shura), as opposed to individual ijtihad.
المجمع الفقهي (al-majma' al-fiqhi): An institution comprising elite jurists from various Islamic countries with a permanent office and annual sessions to research and decide on contemporary issues in light of Sharia principles.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): A method of legal reasoning based on public interest (maslaha) where no explicit text exists, used alongside qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what were the two essential requirements for creating a modern Islamic civil code?
A) Returning to only the Hanafi school and using qiyas.
B) Derivation from all Islamic schools of thought and new deduction using qiyas, istihsan, and istislah.
C) Adopting Egyptian civil code and convening conferences.
D) Closing the door of ijtihad and following only the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Modern fiqh academies operationalize collective ijtihad to address contemporary issues by drawing on all schools and using evidence-based deduction.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

The Revival of Ijtihad: From Individual to Collective
Describes the revival of ijtihad through collective bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasons for the shift from individual to collective ijtihad and describe the role of modern fiqh academies.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad institutionalizes scholarly consultation to address modern issues that individual ijtihad could not handle.

After the closure of ijtihad in the 5th century AH, Islamic jurisprudence risked stagnation. This passage by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains how modern challenges like insurance and joint-stock companies demanded a revival. The solution was to transfer ijtihad from individual scholars to collective bodies—fiqh academies—ensuring both continuity and adaptability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical problem
Recognize that the closure of individual ijtihad around the 5th century AH was a precautionary measure due to fears of error. What were those fears, and why did they lead to closure?
The author mentions 'مخاوف' (fears) that necessitated the precaution.
2
Analyze the modern need
New issues like insurance contracts and joint-stock companies required fresh rulings. Why could classical texts not address them?
These are 'قضايا الساعة' (contemporary issues) and 'أمور مستجدة' (new matters).
3
Understand the remedy: collective ijtihad
The author proposes transferring ijtihad from individuals to a group of trustworthy jurists via shura (consultation). How does this eliminate the earlier drawbacks?
Collective ijtihad is defined as 'الاجتهاد الجماعي' practiced by 'فقهاء العصر الثقات'.
4
Trace the institutional outcome
Identify the three fiqh academies established: Academy of Islamic Research (Al-Azhar), Islamic Fiqh Academy (Muslim World League), and Islamic Fiqh Academy (OIC). What are their structures?
Each has a permanent office and annual sessions.
5
Review examples of their work
The passage lists issues like organ transplants, prayer times near the North Pole, intellectual property, and zakat on modern wealth. How do these illustrate the scope of collective ijtihad?
These are 'موضوعات عُنيت بها' (topics they dealt with).

Chapter (17) The Eighth Period: 1355 AH - Now. Use of qiyas, istihsan, and istislah regarding contemporary issues and new matters that the jurisprudential schools were silent about in their classical texts, such as insurance contracts and joint-stock companies (anonymous), and others. This necessitates practicing ijtihad, as the early jurists did with what they faced of new developments. So the spirit must be breathed into ijtihad anew after its door was closed since the fifth century AH. And if the previous fears at that time of the ijtihad practiced by individuals necessitated that precautionary measure of closing its door, as previously explained, then the remedy is to transfer it from the responsibility of individuals to the responsibility of the group, so ijtihad becomes collective, practiced by trustworthy jurists of the age through mutual consultation among themselves, after it was individual, practiced by each jurist alone. Thus the fruits of ijtihad are achieved, since fiqh cannot meet the needs of the ages without it, and the drawbacks of individual ijtihad that called for closing its door are eliminated. From here grew the idea of establishing a fiqh academy comprising an elite of contemporary jurists from various Islamic countries, with a permanent office and annual session meetings in which research is presented, contemporary issues are discussed, and appropriate solutions are decided in light of the principles of Sharia and its general objectives, and the opinions of earlier jurists and the need of the age. And among the fruits of this call and direction was the convening of conferences of Islamic fiqh, of which we mention: - Islamic Fiqh Week in Damascus in Shawwal 1380 AH (= April 1961 CE). b - Conference of the Academy of Islamic Research at Al-Azhar in Cairo, 1961 CE. c - Conference of the city of Bayda in Libya called by the Libyan University. - Islamic Fiqh Conference in Riyadh in 1396 AH (= 1976 CE) called by Imam Muhammad bin Saud Islamic University. As for the academy, first the (Academy of Islamic Research) of Al-Azhar was established in 1961 CE. Then the Muslim World League in Mecca established (the Islamic Fiqh Academy) and held its first session in Sha'ban 1398 AH mid-1978 CE. Then the Organization of the Islamic Conference established (the Islamic Fiqh Academy) which held its first session in Mecca in 2/1405 AH (11/1984 CE). This academy includes a full member from each state of the Organization of the Islamic Conference. Each of these three fiqh academies has a permanent office, and they hold annual sessions that address important issues to determine the position of Islamic fiqh and its ruling on them, whether permissibility or prohibition. Examples of topics that these academies have dealt with and issued decisions on: - Transplantation of human organs, artificial insemination, and test-tube babies. - Determining prayer times in countries near the North Pole and the beginnings of lunar months. - Rights of literary and intellectual property and expropriation for public purposes. - Zakat on modern forms of wealth. - Very many decisions related to economic matters, Islamic banks, and financial markets. These academies publish scientific journals containing the research submitted to the academy sessions, the discussions, and the decisions issued. 250.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the eighth and current period of Islamic fiqh development, beginning around 1355 AH. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, describes how modern issues require renewed ijtihad using tools like qiyas, istihsan, and istislah. He notes that the closure of individual ijtihad in the 5th century AH was a precaution, but now the solution is collective ijtihad by trustworthy scholars through consultation. This led to the establishment of three major fiqh academies: the Academy of Islamic Research (Al-Azhar), the Islamic Fiqh Academy (Muslim World League), and the Islamic Fiqh Academy (OIC). The passage lists examples of contemporary issues they address, such as organ transplants, prayer times near the poles, intellectual property, and modern financial transactions. The author emphasizes that these academies have permanent offices and annual sessions, producing research, discussions, and decisions. This passage highlights the shift from individual to institutional ijtihad as a response to modern challenges.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihad al-jamā'ī): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of trustworthy jurists through consultation (shura) rather than by individuals alone.
المجمع الفقهي (al-majma' al-fiqhī): A fiqh academy comprising elite jurists from various Islamic countries, with a permanent office and annual sessions to discuss and issue rulings on contemporary issues.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): A method of legal reasoning that considers public interest (maslaha) in the absence of explicit textual evidence.
الشورى (al-shūrā): Mutual consultation among scholars, a principle emphasized in the passage as the method for collective ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the main reason for shifting from individual to collective ijtihad according to the passage?
A) To increase the number of fatwas issued.
B) To eliminate the drawbacks of individual ijtihad that led to its closure.
C) Because modern scholars are less capable than earlier ones.
D) To reduce the influence of non-Hanafi schools.

Key Takeaway
The shift to collective ijtihad through fiqh academies is the primary modern method to address contemporary legal challenges within Islamic jurisprudence.

Openness to Madhhabs and Comparative Fiqh in Modern Education
Historical discussion of modern university curriculum reforms in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the modern shift towards openness to all madhhabs in university fiqh education and explain how it influences the process of selection for codification (taqnin).

Core Idea
Openness to all madhhabs eliminates fanaticism and enables selection of the best ruling for each time and place.

This passage from Chapter Three of Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's work describes a pivotal shift in modern fiqh education. Traditional madhhab texts were replaced by comparative approaches that study all schools, including early jurists. This openness is not about abandoning one's madhhab but about enriching the juristic toolkit for contemporary application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Old madhhab-based texts were unsuited for modern university teaching; they were designed for traditional study or as scholarly references.
Consider why traditional texts fail modern pedagogy: they assume prior knowledge and do not compare schools.
2
Recognize the solution
Professors authored modern textbooks tailored to bachelor's and PhD levels, covering diverse topics including constitutional and economic fiqh.
This shows the expansion of fiqh into new fields.
3
Understand the scope of openness
Openness includes all four Sunni madhhabs, four other schools (e.g., Zahiri, Jabari?), and early jurists whose opinions are preserved in books of ikhtilaf.
The passage mentions al-Awza'i, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, etc.
4
Grasp the consequence
This openness eliminated madhhab fanaticism that existed in the past.
Fanaticism prevented objective study and restricted benefit from other schools.
5
Apply to contemporary practice
Students now learn to compare merits of different madhhabs, enabling them to select the most suitable ruling for temporal and spatial needs when codifying Islamic law.
This is directly relevant for muftis and legislators.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
طوراً جديداً. ذلك أن الكتب المذهبية القديمة التي كانت مؤلفة للتعليم بأسلوبه القديم أو كمرجع للعلماء، ليست ملائمة لحاجة التدريس الجامعي في هذا العصر فانبرى أساتذة الفقه في كليات الشريعة إلى وضع مؤلفات حديثة في الفقه لملاءمة حاجة الطلاب في مراحل الدراسة العالية والعليا. فلمرحلة الإجازة (البكالوريوس) أسلوب تعليمي ولمرحلة الدكتوراة أسلوب تعمقي في موضوعات خاصة وبذلك وجدت رسائل ماجستير ودكتوراه في موضوعات فقهية كثيرة، تستوعب كل موضوع وتناقشه بتعمق من مختلف جوانبه، وتغني الباحثين والمراجعين، وأصبحت تعد بالعشرات، وبعضها في الفقه الدستوري كنظام الحكم والشورى، وفي الفقه الاقتصادي، وحقوق السلم والحرب في الإسلام (القانون الدولي الإسلامي)، وفي حقوق الإنسان، وحقوق المرأة.
١٧ / ٤ - الانفتاح على المذاهب، والاهتمام بالفقه المقارن. إن التجديد في تدريس الفقه والتأليف فيه في الجامعات قد صاحبه الانفتاح على المذاهب الفقهية جميعاً، الأربعة الممثلة لفقه أهل السنة والأربعة الأخرى التي سبقت الإشارة إليها في موضعها بل وعلى آراء الصحابة والتابعين وسائر فقهاء السلف الذين لم تدوّن مذاهبهم تدويناً كاملاً وإنما نقلت لهم آراء في موضوعات شتى في كتب اختلاف الفقهاء كالأوزاعي والليث بن سعد وابن شبرمة وابن أبي ليلى، والحسن البصري وسفيان الثوري وغيرهم للاستفادة من آرائهم فيما نقل عنهم .. هذا الانفتاح قد أزال العصبية المذهبية التي كانت بين أتباع المذاهب في الماضي مما سبقت الإشارة إليه وأصبحت الدراسة الفقهية في الجامعات تُظهر للطالب مزايا المذاهب المختلفة وما فيها من ثروة فقهية، وتنمية فكرية وتوسعة للمدارك بمناقشة الأدلة، وتيسير على المكلف في التطبيق، وتسهيل على أولياء الأمور لعملية التقنين من الفقه الإسلامي أن يختاروا في كل موضوع من كل مذهب ما هو أنسب وأوفى بالحاجة الزمنية والمكانية.
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics. A new phase. That is because the old madhhab-based books, which were written for teaching in the old style or as references for scholars, are not suitable for the needs of university teaching in this era. So the professors of fiqh in the Sharia colleges stepped forward to write modern works in fiqh to suit the needs of students in higher and advanced stages of study. For the bachelor's stage, an educational method, and for the doctorate stage, an in-depth method in specialized topics. Thus, master's and doctoral theses appeared on many fiqh topics, covering each topic and discussing it in depth from various aspects, enriching researchers and reviewers, and they are counted by the dozens. Some of them are in constitutional fiqh like the system of government and consultation, in economic fiqh, rights of peace and war in Islam (Islamic international law), and in human rights and women's rights. 17/4 - Openness to Madhhabs, and Interest in Comparative Fiqh. Indeed, the renewal in teaching fiqh and authoring in it in universities was accompanied by openness to all fiqh madhhabs, the four representing the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah and the four others mentioned earlier in their place, and even to the opinions of the Companions, the Followers, and all the early jurists whose madhhabs were not fully recorded, but opinions are transmitted from them on various topics in books of scholarly differences, such as al-Awza'i, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Ibn Shubruma, Ibn Abi Layla, al-Hasan al-Basri, Sufyan al-Thawri, and others, to benefit from their opinions as transmitted from them. This openness has eliminated the madhhab fanaticism that existed among followers of madhhabs in the past, as previously indicated. The study of fiqh in universities now shows the student the merits of different madhhabs and the fiqh wealth they contain, intellectual development, broadening of understanding by discussing evidence, making it easier for the legally responsible person in application, and facilitating for those in authority the process of codification from Islamic fiqh to choose in each topic from every madhhab what is most suitable and fulfills the temporal and spatial need.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from Chapter Three discusses the modern period of fiqh development. The author notes that traditional madhhab-based texts were not suited for university teaching, prompting professors to create modern works. The key point is the openness to all madhhabs – the four Sunni schools and four others, as well as early jurists – which helped eliminate past fanaticism. Students now learn the merits of different madhhabs and how to use them for codification according to time and place. This approach highlights comparative fiqh as a means of renewal.

Key Terms
الفقه المقارن (al-fiqh al-muqarin): Comparative fiqh, the study of multiple madhhabs' positions on a single issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence of the openness to all madhhabs in university fiqh education according to the passage?
A) Increased fanaticism
B) Elimination of madhhab fanaticism
C) Confusion among students
D) Rejection of early jurists

Key Takeaway
Modern university fiqh education embraces all madhhabs and early jurists to provide a rich source for codification and contemporary application.

The Contemporary Turn: Utilizing All Madhhabs
Argument for comprehensive fiqh utilization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author argues that Islamic jurisprudence as a whole is sufficient for modern legislative needs while individual madhhabs may be limited.

Core Idea
The richness of Islamic fiqh lies in its collective schools, not in any single madhhab alone.

This lesson explores a key contemporary trend: moving beyond adherence to a single madhhab and drawing from the entire corpus of Islamic jurisprudence. The passage argues that what a single school cannot address, another school can, making the collective fiqh heritage a vast resource for modern codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The passage states that thinkers find the limitation in each individual school, not in Islamic fiqh overall. Read the text to see this contrast.
The author uses the phrase 'الضيق عن الحاجات التشريعية العصرية ليس هو في الفقه الإسلامي عامة'.
2
Recognize the solution
The solution is to look beyond one madhhab to all madhhabs and early jurists. The passage encourages a comprehensive view.
3
Understand the basis
All schools have equal relation to Sharia, so all opinions are legitimate sources. This legitimizes using multiple schools.
The key phrase is 'نسبتها إلى الشريعة الإسلامية متساوية'.
4
Appreciate the implication
This approach allows for comprehensive and flexible legislation. Modern codification can draw from the full fiqh heritage.

Chapter Four: On Contemporary Codification Deriving from Islamic Jurisprudence. Chapter Eighteen: The Contemporary Direction for Benefiting from All Islamic Jurisprudence with All Its Schools. 1/18 - Today in many countries of the Arab and Islamic world, there appears a turn towards Islamic jurisprudence as a whole to benefit from its schools. And the thinkers who have studied modern law and have examined the positive local laws in these Arab countries and many European civil laws, when they cast their gaze beyond the Hanafi or Shafi'i school or others, and compare those laws with what the collection of Islamic ijtihads has produced of opinions, theories, and fertile fiqh, they find that the narrowness regarding contemporary legislative needs is not in Islamic jurisprudence generally, but rather in each individual school separately. What a single school and its theories fall short of, another school has expanse and treatment for it. And there has been no legislation in which ijtihads have multiplied and opinions expanded like Islamic legislation. And these schools - whether those fully codified among the four previously mentioned, or those not fully codified but whose opinions have been correctly transmitted in books of juristic differences - all these schools have equal relation to Islamic Sharia. So the opinions of the jurists of the Companions and after them the Successors and their successors, like Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Shubruma, Makhul, al-Awza'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, 259.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Four of the book, which discusses contemporary codification derived from Islamic jurisprudence. The author first outlines the structure of the chapter, listing the topics to be covered: the contemporary trend to use all madhhabs, the importance of differing ijtihad, the religious character of fiqh, modern problems in applying Sharia, and the role of civil laws in Islamic countries. Then, in Chapter Eighteen, he explains the contemporary turn towards utilizing all Islamic legal schools together. He observes that thinkers who study modern legal systems and compare them with the rich outputs of Islamic ijtihad find that the limitation for modern legislative needs is not in Islamic jurisprudence as a whole, but in each individual school taken separately. The solution lies in drawing from all schools, including not only the four well-known ones but also the opinions of early jurists like Companions and Successors (e.g., Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Shubruma, Makhul, al-Awza'i, al-Hasan al-Basri). All these have equal standing relative to the Sharia. This approach advocates for a comprehensive engagement with the entire Islamic juristic heritage to meet contemporary challenges.

Key Terms
مذاهب (madhaahib): Schools of Islamic jurisprudence, including fully compiled schools and those whose views are recorded in books of ikhtilaf.
فقه إسلامي (fiqh islaamiy): The entire body of Islamic jurisprudence, encompassing all opinions and theories from all schools and early jurists.
شريعة إسلامية (shari'ah islaamiyyah): The divine Islamic law, from which all madhhabs derive and to which they have equal relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do thinkers find a narrowness in positive laws compared to Islamic jurisprudence?
A) Because positive laws are secular.
B) Because they only look at one madhhab.
C) Because Islamic jurisprudence is incomplete.
D) Because modern laws are too complex.

Key Takeaway
Islamic jurisprudence as a whole is rich and comprehensive; the key is to draw from all its schools, not just one.

The Equal Standing of All Madhhabs
Principle of equal authority of all madhhabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that all madhhabs have equal relation to Sharia and identify the scope of schools including the opinions of Companions and early jurists.

Core Idea
All schools of Islamic jurisprudence are equally rooted in Sharia; no school holds a monopoly on divine truth.

This lesson focuses on a fundamental claim in the passage: that all madhhabs, whether fully codified or transmitted through ikhtilaf books, have an equal relationship to the Islamic Sharia. This principle legitimizes the use of opinions from early jurists like Ibn Abi Layla, al-Awza'i, and others, alongside the four well-known schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage says 'all these schools have equal relation to Sharia'. Read the text to find this phrase.
The key Arabic phrase is: 'هذه المذاهب كلها نسبتها إلى الشريعة الإسلامية متساوية'.
2
Understand the scope
This includes not only the four codified schools but also opinions of Companions, Successors, and later jurists whose views are preserved. Note the names listed.
The passage mentions Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Shubruma, Makhul, al-Awza'i, al-Hasan al-Basri.
3
Recognize the implication
No single school is inherently superior or more authoritative than another. They all derive from the same divine source.
4
Apply the principle
In fatwa and legislation, one can adopt opinions from any of these sources as long as they are authentically transmitted.
The condition of authentic transmission is crucial.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
15 min
The Principle of Benefiting from All Fiqh Schools
Explains the modern approach to drawing from all Islamic legal schools for legislation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of utilizing multiple fiqh schools in modern legislation and identify the key considerations that guided historical examples like the Majalla and Ottoman Family Law.

Core Idea
Islamic law's richness is in its collective ijtihad; no single school suffices for all modern needs.

This lesson explores a pivotal shift in legal methodology: moving from exclusive adherence to one madhhab to selectively drawing from all schools to address contemporary legislative needs. We will examine the rationale behind this trend and two landmark examples: the Majalla’s evaluation of Ibn Shubruma’s view on conditions, and the Ottoman Family Law’s adoption of Maliki arbitration rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize that modern legislative needs often exceed the capacity of any single fiqh school. The passage notes that narrowness is in individual schools, not in Islamic fiqh as a whole.
This insight shifts focus from school loyalty to the broader tradition.
2
Understand the Solution: Talfiq and Takhayyur
The solution is to benefit from all schools, taking rulings from different madhahib as needed. The author emphasizes that all madhahib have equal value relative to Sharia.
This is not arbitrary cherry-picking but principled selection.
3
Analyze the Majalla Example
The Majalla committee considered Ibn Shubruma's view on absolute freedom of conditions but opted to stay within Hanafi fiqh because Hanafi principles already validate customary conditions unless contradictory to a text or Sharia objectives.
Note the criterion: necessity and compatibility with Sharia.
4
Analyze the Ottoman Family Law Example
The Ottoman government codified family law by adopting Maliki rulings on compulsory judicial separation through arbitration, enabling women to seek divorce. This shows practical selection from another school to address a social need.
The law also took freedom of marriage from the Maliki school.

Source Text
