الفصل الثامن عشر
الاتجاه العصري للاستفادة كافة من الفقه الإسلامي بجميع مذاهبه
۱/۱۸ - تبدو اليوم في كثير من البلاد من العالم العربي والإسلامي، التفاتة نحو الفقه الإسلامي بأجمعه للاستفادة من مذاهبه.
والمفكرون الذين درسوا الحقوق الحديثة واطلعوا على القوانين الوضعية المحلية في هذه الأقطار العربية وعلى كثير من القوانين المدنية الأوروبية، عندما يلقون بأبصارهم إلى أبعد من المذهب الحنفي أو الشافعي أو غيرهما، ويقارنون تلك القوانين بما أوجدته مجموعة الاجتهادات الإسلامية من آراء ونظريات وفقه خصيب يجدون أن الضيق عن الحاجات التشريعية العصرية ليس هو في الفقه الإسلامي عامة، وإنما هو في كل مذهب فردي على حدة.
وما يضيق عنه المذهب الواحد ونظرياته ففي مذهب آخر سَعَة منه وعلاج. ولم يوجد تشريع كَثُرَت فيه الاجتهادات واتسعت الآراء كالتشريع الإسلامي.
وهذه المذاهب - سواء منها الأربعة المدوّنة بكاملها التي سلف ذكرها وما كان منها لم يدوّن بكامله، وإنما نقلت آراء أصحابه في كتب الاختلاف الفقهي نقلاً صحيحاً - هذه المذاهب كلها نسبتها إلى الشريعة الإسلامية متساوية. فآراء فقهاء الصحابة ومن بعدهم من التابعين وتابعيهم، كابن أبي ليلى وابن شبرمة، ومكحول والأوزاعي، والحسن البصري، وسعيد بن جبير، وكثير غيرهم لها من القيمة والاعتبار ما لآراء أبي حنيفة ومالك، والشافعي، وابن حنبل.
وليس من المحتم أن يأخذ كل قطر مذهب أحد المجتهدين بكامله بل يمكن أن يأخذ من قواعد كل مذهب وأحكامه ما يرى أنه الأليق بالمصالح الزمنية.
نفسها.
وقد ظهرت هذه الحاجة إلى فقه المذاهب الأخرى لجمعية المجلة.
ويشعر بذلك ما جاء في تقرير لائحة الأسباب الموجبة التي صدرت بها المجلة من بحث الجمعية المذكورة عن مذهب ابن شبرمة في اعتبار الشروط مطلقاً في العقود، ومن المناقشات التي جرت حول الأخذ بمذهبه، ثم ترجيحها الاقتصار في ذلك على المذهب الحنفي لتوسطه ولعدم الحاجة (في نظرهم) إلى الأخذ بمذهب ابن شبرمة لأن الاجتهاد الحنفي يعتبر ويصحح كل شرط جرى عليه العرف إذا لم يكن هناك نص يمنعه بخصوصه ولا ينافي مقاصد الشريعة العامة.
۲/۱۸ - بدء تنفيذ الفكرة في تقنين الأحوال الشخصية:
ثم بدىء رسمياً بتنفيذ هذه الفكرة فكرة الاستفادة من مختلف المذاهب الفقهية عن طريق التقنين في أحكام الأحوال الشخصية أواخر العهد العثماني، إذ وضعت الحكومة العثمانية قانون حقوق العائلة في سنة ۱۳۳۳ رومية وأخذت فيه من المذهب المالكي حكم التفريق الإجباري القضائي بين الزوجين عن طريق التحكيم المنصوص عليه في القرآن عند اختلافهما، وتوسعت فيه. وبذلك مكنت المرأة أن تتخلص من زوج السوء بطلبها التفريق كما يتمكن الرجل أن يتخلص من امرأة السوء بالتطليق، كما أخذت أحكاماً إصلاحية أخرى من مذاهب أخرى.
وقد أخذ القانون المذكور أيضاً من مذهب مالك إطلاق حرية الزواج
٢٦٠
Chapter Eighteen: The Contemporary Trend of Benefiting Fully from Islamic Fiqh in All Its Schools. 1/18 - Today, in many countries of the Arab and Islamic world, there is an inclination towards Islamic fiqh as a whole to benefit from its schools. And the thinkers who have studied modern rights and have seen the local positive laws in these Arab countries and many European civil laws, when they cast their gaze beyond the Hanafi or Shafi'i or other schools, and compare those laws with what the collection of Islamic ijtihads has produced of opinions, theories, and fruitful fiqh, they find that the narrowness regarding contemporary legislative needs is not in Islamic fiqh in general, but rather in each individual school separately. And what a single school and its theories are insufficient for, another school has ample scope and remedy for. No legislation has seen as many ijtihads and as wide opinions as Islamic legislation. And these madhhabs - whether the four fully compiled ones mentioned earlier or those not fully compiled but whose founders' opinions have been correctly transmitted in books of fiqh differences - all these madhhabs have equal relation to the Islamic Sharia. So the opinions of the jurists among the Companions and those after them from the Followers and their successors, such as Ibn Abi Layla, Ibn Shubruma, Makhul, al-Awza'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and many others, have the same value and consideration as the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal. And it is not necessary for every country to adopt the madhhab of one mujtahid in its entirety; rather, it can take from the principles and rulings of each madhhab what it deems most suitable for the temporal interests. The same need for fiqh of other schools appeared for the compilation of the Majalla. This is felt in the report of the statement of reasons issued with the Majalla, from the investigation of the said committee into the madhhab of Ibn Shubruma regarding the absolute consideration of conditions in contracts, and the discussions that took place about adopting his madhhab, and then their preference to limit that to the Hanafi madhhab because of its moderation and because (in their view) there was no need to adopt Ibn Shubruma's madhhab since Hanafi ijtihad considers and validates every condition that has become customary if there is no specific text prohibiting it and it does not contradict the general objectives of the Sharia. 2/18 - Beginning of the Implementation of the Idea in Codifying Personal Status: Then the implementation of this idea - the idea of benefiting from various fiqh schools through codification in personal status rulings - officially began at the end of the Ottoman era, when the Ottoman government enacted the Law of Family Rights in the year 1333 Rumi and took from the Maliki school the ruling of compulsory judicial separation between spouses through arbitration as stipulated in the Quran when they differ, and expanded upon it. Thus, it enabled a woman to rid herself of a bad husband by requesting separation just as a man can rid himself of a bad wife by divorce, and it also took other reformative rulings from other schools. The said law also took from the Maliki school the openness of the freedom of marriage. 260
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of legal philosophy and methodology, addressing the contemporary trend of utilizing all Islamic fiqh schools for legislative purposes. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, argues that the richness of Islamic jurisprudence lies in its collective ijtihad across schools, not in any single school. He notes that thinkers who compare modern positive laws with Islamic fiqh find that limitations are per-school, not per the entire tradition. He emphasizes that all madhhabs, including those of early jurists like Ibn Abi Layla and Ibn Shubruma, have equal value relative to Sharia. He then illustrates with two examples: first, the Majalla committee's consideration of Ibn Shubruma's view on conditions in contracts, which they ultimately set aside because Hanafi fiqh already accommodates customary conditions; second, the Ottoman Law of Family Rights (1917) which adopted Maliki rules on judicial separation through arbitration and freedom of marriage. This demonstrates the practical application of choosing from different madhhabs to meet modern needs. The passage underscores the principle that codification can selectively take from various schools without binding a country to one madhhab entirely. The authority weight is that of a contemporary jurist's analysis, not a classical ruling.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnīn): The act of codifying legal rulings into a formal legislative code.
الأحوال الشخصية (al-aḥwāl al-shakhṣiyya): Personal status law, covering marriage, divorce, inheritance, etc.
التحكيم (al-taḥkīm): Arbitration, specifically the Quranic process of appointing arbiters from both families to resolve marital disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Majalla committee ultimately not adopt Ibn Shubruma's view on conditions in contracts?
A) They considered Ibn Shubruma not a reliable jurist.
B) Hanafi fiqh already validates customary conditions if not against a text or general objectives.
C) They wanted to limit the scope of the Majalla to purely Hanafi rulings.
D) They found the opinion too liberal.

Key Takeaway
The principle of utilizing all fiqh schools is a legitimate method for modern legislation, guided by necessity and compatibility with Sharia objectives, as demonstrated by the Majalla and Ottoman Family Law.

Principle of Codification from Multiple Madhahib
States the principle of cross-madhhab codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of cross-madhhab codification and apply it to identify permissible sources for modern legislation.

Core Idea
Modern codification may draw from any madhhab's rulings, not just one.

This passage marks a foundational shift: the four madhahib are not exclusive silos for legislation. The author argues that the opinions of early jurists like Sa'id ibn Jubayr are equally authoritative, and that a legislator may select from each school what best serves contemporary interests. This principle underlies much of modern Islamic legal reform.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional assumption
Many assume codification must follow one madhhab entirely. What is that assumption?
The passage challenges this by stating it is not necessary.
2
State the new principle
The author rejects the assumption of exclusive adoption. State the alternative: selection from multiple schools based on suitability.
3
Note the justification
The value of early jurists (like Sa'id ibn Jubayr) is equal to the four imams, so their opinions are also viable sources.
This broadens the pool of authoritative opinions.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
وسعيد بن جبير، وكثير غيرهم لها من القيمة والاعتبار ما لآراء أبي حنيفة ومالك، والشافعي، وابن حنبل .

Chapter Four: The derivation of contemporary codification from fiqh. And Sa'id ibn Jubayr and many others have the same value and consideration as the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal. And it is not necessary that every country adopt the madhhab of one of the mujtahids in its entirety; rather, it may take from the principles and rulings of each madhhab what it deems most suitable for temporal interests. The same.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth chapter on how modern legal codification draws from classical fiqh. The author asserts that the opinions of early jurists like Sa'id ibn Jubayr hold no less value than those of the four imams, implying that codifiers are not restricted to a single madhhab. The key principle is that a legislature may select from any madhhab's rules whatever serves contemporary needs, without being bound to adopt one school in full. This sets the philosophical foundation for the subsequent examples of cross-madhhab borrowing in Ottoman and Egyptian law.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh into statutory law.
المذهب (al-madhhab): A school of Islamic jurisprudence (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main principle regarding modern codification from fiqh?
A) Each country must adopt one madhhab in its entirety.
B) Codification may draw from multiple madhahib, selecting rules best suited to contemporary interests.
C) Only the four imams' opinions are valid for codification.
D) Countries must follow the majority school of their region.

Key Takeaway
Modern codification of Islamic law can permissibly select rules from different madhahib based on temporal interests, without being bound to one school.

Egyptian Reforms on Conditional and Triple Divorce
Explaining Egyptian legislative reforms based on broader fiqh opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between divorce intended as an oath and divorce intended to dissolve marriage, and evaluate the Hanafi position versus the reform position on triple divorce.

Core Idea
A divorce not intended to end marriage is void under Egyptian law—a departure from the Hanafi school.

This passage from al-Zarqa’s al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi examines the Egyptian legislative reforms on conditional and triple divorce. It explains how modern legislation adopted opinions from beyond the four schools to prevent misuse of divorce, focusing on the intent (qasd) of the husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of divorce addressed
The passage discusses conditional divorce (talāq mu‘allaq) where divorce is suspended on a condition, and triple divorce (talāq thalāth) pronounced in one utterance. Note how each is treated differently in the reform.
The reform targets misuse, not all forms of conditional divorce.
2
Distinguish the Hanafi ruling from the reform ruling
In the Hanafi school, conditional divorce takes effect upon fulfillment of the condition, regardless of intent. The Egyptian Law No. 25 of 1929 nullifies it if the intent was only to urge or prevent, not to divorce. Similarly, triple divorce counts as one, not three.
The reform relies on Ibn Taymiyyah’s opinion, not a classical Hanafi view.
3
Examine the rationale: purpose (qasd) of divorce
The author argues that using divorce as a threat or oath is a deviation from its legislated purpose (dissolution when marital life is impossible). The law aims to restore the original maqasid.
This is a teleological (maqasidi) argument.
4
Apply the legal definition dissector to conditional divorce under the reform
Use the dissector: the hukm is that it does not take effect; the sabab is suspending divorce on a condition with intent to urge/prevent; the shart is that the intent was not actual divorce; the mani is the real intent to divorce; the athar is the divorce is void.
This dissector is based on the reform perspective, not the classical Hanafi.
5
Note the tarjih mechanism: why adopt a non-Hanafi opinion?
The passage states the reform was enacted to eliminate tragedies, with approval of Al-Azhar. The author later defends this by istihsan/istislah if no explicit precedent existed. This shows that modern legislation can choose from any school based on maslaha.
This is a key principle of modern talfiq in Muslim family law.

Chapter (18) The Modern Trend of Benefiting from Fiqh. The wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance, while the Hanafi madhhab rules waiting for the death of all his peers in age, so the wife of the missing remains suspended until her old age. 3/18 - Then the Egyptian government followed this approach in the case of separation and the wife of the missing in the year 1920 CE. Then in 1929 CE the Egyptian government took a wide step in adopting various ijtihads beyond the four madhhabs, and issued Law No. 25 in which it cancelled conditional divorce in most of its cases, and also considered divorcing three or two with one utterance as one divorce, acting on the opinion of Ibn Taymiyyah and its legal basis, with the approval of Al-Azhar to get rid of the tragedies of suspended divorce and triple divorce, which are committed by ignorant and foolish men in moments of conflict or anger, thus stepping outside the bounds of the Sunnah and the fundamental objectives of legislated divorce, then they seek an escape from it through various despicable tricks and means after its occurrence, as known by the people of knowledge. (1) Divorce has been legislated in Islam as a way of dissolving marriage when good companionship between spouses is impossible. So if a husband suspends divorce, for example by saying: if you do not do such and such, or if such and such matter has not happened, then my wife is divorced, he has used divorce to strengthen his resolve to do something, or to confirm a news in place of swearing by Allah, because for these confirmations swearing by Allah has been legislated, not divorce. Thus the man has done wrong by using divorce for other than what it was legislated for, and thus deviated from its legal objectives. Most divorce tragedies occur through conditional divorce hanging on their own actions or those of their wives or others, such as eating, drinking, coming, going, visiting, speaking to someone, wearing a garment, etc., for the sake of urging the action or preventing it, depending on whether the condition is negative or positive, like: if you do such and such, or if I do not do. Rather, they often suspend divorce on actions between them and their friends or enemies, in which their wives have no involvement. Page 249, Chapter Four: Derivation of Modern Codification from Fiqh. 4/18 - Some thinkers among contemporary scholars hold that the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be considered as one large madhhab in the Sharia, and each individual madhhab among them, like the Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali, and others, is considered in this large general madhhab as different opinions and statements within a single individual madhhab. Thus the scholars of the Ummah weigh and choose from them for codification in the field of judiciary and fatwa what is most fulfilling of the temporal need and the requirements of maslaha in every era. This is a sound opinion. = (1) in it, so the condition upon which it was suspended occurs, and the woman is surprised by a divorce that disrupts the family, having done nothing to deserve it nor having any knowledge of it. And the suspended divorce may be three, after which there is no possibility of return. All this originates from people's deviation in divorce from its legal style and purpose since ancient times, and their using it in a suspended form to strengthen resolve to do or leave something, instead of swearing by Allah, for other than what divorce was legislated for, even contrary to its legal purpose which the Quranic texts indicate its limits. Scholars are heedless of this and surrendered to people's whims and ignorance in changing the legitimate way in the most dangerous matter. The Egyptian country has gotten rid of these tragedies by prohibiting suspended divorce and nullifying it in judiciary if the intention behind its suspension was only urging or preventing (i.e., incitement to do or leave). This measure in Egypt, even if it had no basis in the old fiqh madhhabs that Ibn al-Qayyim transmitted in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in from senior Companions, it would have been incumbent upon today's scholars of the Ummah to adopt it by way of istihsan or istislah, to return people to the legitimate path in using divorce and to put an end to the tragedies of their ignorance in it. (1) Finally, in Syria in 1953 CE (after the third edition of this Fiqh Introduction), a unified personal status law was issued encompassing all family law rulings in its four sections: marriage and its branches: alimony, lineage, custody, divorce, separation, iddah, etc.; capacity and guardianship and its branches: legal representation for minors in all its forms - like guardianship, custodianship, etc.; will and its conditions and rulings; inheritance. And I was one of the members of the official committee entrusted by the Syrian government with its drafting. So we derived its rulings from Islamic fiqh with all its madhhabs and opinions, and we adopted all the reformative rulings that had been taken by the Ottoman Family Rights Law before, and the laws -
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm discusses modern legislative utilization of Islamic fiqh, specifically addressing problems with conditional divorce (talāq mu‘allaq) and triple divorce in one utterance. The author describes the Egyptian legislative reforms of 1920 and 1929, which adopted opinions from beyond the four schools—notably Ibn Taymiyyah’s view that triple divorce counts as one and that conditional divorce not intended to effect divorce but only to urge or prevent is ineffective. These reforms were approved by Al-Azhar and aim to prevent the tragedies resulting from misuse of divorce. The author explains that divorce is legislated only as a last resort when marital life becomes impossible; using it as a threat or to emphasize a statement is a misapplication. The footnote (1) elaborates on the nature of conditional divorce and its deviation from legal objectives. The passage then transitions (page 249) to a broader philosophical point: the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be treated as one large school, allowing selection from any opinion that best serves the need of the time and maslaha. The author supports this as a sound view (ra’yun sadīd). He further defends the Egyptian measure by noting that even if it had no explicit basis in the early schools, it would be justified by istihsan or istislah. Finally, the footnote (1) mentions the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953, in which the author participated, which derived its provisions from all madhhabs and earlier reformist laws.

Key Terms
الطلاق المعلق (al-ṭalāq al-mu‘allaq): طلاق يعلق على شرط، مثل 'إن فعلت كذا فأنت طالق'، وكان القصد منه الحث على فعل أو المنع منه لا إنهاء الزواج.
الطلاق الثلاث (al-ṭalāq al-thalāth): طلاق الثلاث بكلمة واحدة أو كلمات متصلة في مجلس واحد.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما الأساس الشرعي الذي اعتمد عليه القانون المصري رقم ٢٥ لسنة ١٩٢٩ في اعتبار الطلاق الثلاث بلفظ واحد طلقة واحدة؟
A) رأي الإمام أبي حنيفة
B) رأي ابن تيمية ومستنده الشرعي
C) الإجماع
D) القياس على الطلاق الرجعي

Key Takeaway
Modern legislation may depart from the dominant madhhab when maslaha requires, and intent (qasd) is key in conditional divorce.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
7 min
Treating All Madhhabs as One: A Principle for Codification
Explaining a methodological principle for codification from fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that all ijtihadi madhhabs can be treated as one large school, and evaluate its implications for modern fatwa and legislation.

Core Idea
Treat all madhhabs as opinions within one large school to choose the best for each time.

This passage advances a methodological principle: the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be considered a single vast madhhab, and each actual school (Hanafi, Maliki, etc.) is like an opinion within it. This allows scholars to choose for codification whatever best suits contemporary needs. The author, al-Zarqa, calls this a 'sound opinion' and uses it to justify adopting non-Hanafi views in Egyptian reforms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle stated
The principle: 'مجموعة المذاهب الاجتهادية يجب أن تُعتبر كمذهب واحد كبير في الشريعة' (the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be considered as one large madhhab in Sharia). Each actual school is like a set of views within that large madhhab.
This is a modern reformist idea, not a classical Hanafi principle.
2
Understand the justification
The justification: just as within a single school there are multiple opinions (like the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), so across schools we have a wider pool. Scholars can then 'tarjih' (weigh) and select the most suitable for the age's needs and maslaha.
This extends the concept of tarjih from intra-school to inter-school.
3
Relate to the Egyptian reform example
The author had earlier described Egypt adopting Ibn Taymiyyah's view on conditional and triple divorce, which is outside the Hanafi school. This principle justifies that adoption: the chosen view is simply the preferred opinion within the 'large madhhab'.
Islamic law becomes a pool from which the legislator can pick.
4
Evaluate the implications for fatwa
For a mufti in a jurisdiction that applies this principle, he may give fatwa according to any school that serves maslaha, not necessarily his own school. This changes the traditional mufti's commitment to a single madhhab.
This is a shift from taqlid to a more eclectic approach.
5
Note the author's support via istihsan/istislah
The author argues that even if the reform had no explicit precedent, it would be justified by istihsan or istislah. This shows that the principle is grounded in recognized methods of legal reasoning.
Istihsan and istislah allow departure from qiyas for public good.

٣/١٨ - ثم حذت الحكومة المصرية في قضية التفريق وزوجة المفقود هذا الحذو في سنة ١٩٢٠م.
ثم في سنة ١٩٢٩م خَطَت الحكومة المصرية خطوة واسعة في الأخذ بمختلف الاجتهادات مما وراء المذاهب الأربعة، فأصدرت قانوناً تحت رقم ٢٥ ألغت فيه تعليق الطلاق بالشرط في معظم حالاته، كما اعتبرت تطليق الثلاث أو الثنتين بلفظ واحد طلقة واحدة عملاً برأي ابن تيمية ومستنده الشرعي، وذلك بإقرار مشيخة الأزهر للتخلص من مآسي الطلاق المعلق وطلاق الثلاث، مما يرتكبه جُهَّال الرجال وحمقاهم في ساعات النزاع أو الغضب فيخرجون عن حدود السنة والمقاصد الأساسية في الطلاق المشروع (١)، ثم يلتمسون المَخْرَج منه بشتى الحيل والوسائل الدنيئة بعد الوقوع، مما يعرفه أهل العلم.

فيكون الرجل بذلك قد أساء باستعمال الطلاق في غير ما شرع له، وخرج به عن مقاصده الشرعية.
ومعظم مآسي الطلاق إنما يقع فيها الناس بطريق تعليق الطلاق على أفعالهم أو أفعال نسائهم أو غيرهن من أكل أو شرب أو ذهاب أو إياب أو زيارة أو تكليم شخص أو لبس ثوب إلخ .. لأجل الحمل على الفعل أو المنع عنه، بحسب كون الشرط المعلق عليه من هذه الأفعال سلبياً أو إيجابياً نحو : إن فعلت كذا، أو إن لم أفعل). بل كثيراً ما يعلّقون الطلاق على أفعال بينهم وبين أصدقائهم أو أعدائهم لا دخل لنسائهم .

Chapter (18) The Modern Trend of Benefiting from Fiqh. The wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance, while the Hanafi madhhab rules waiting for the death of all his peers in age, so the wife of the missing remains suspended until her old age. 3/18 - Then the Egyptian government followed this approach in the case of separation and the wife of the missing in the year 1920 CE. Then in 1929 CE the Egyptian government took a wide step in adopting various ijtihads beyond the four madhhabs, and issued Law No. 25 in which it cancelled conditional divorce in most of its cases, and also considered divorcing three or two with one utterance as one divorce, acting on the opinion of Ibn Taymiyyah and its legal basis, with the approval of Al-Azhar to get rid of the tragedies of suspended divorce and triple divorce, which are committed by ignorant and foolish men in moments of conflict or anger, thus stepping outside the bounds of the Sunnah and the fundamental objectives of legislated divorce, then they seek an escape from it through various despicable tricks and means after its occurrence, as known by the people of knowledge. (1) Divorce has been legislated in Islam as a way of dissolving marriage when good companionship between spouses is impossible. So if a husband suspends divorce, for example by saying: if you do not do such and such, or if such and such matter has not happened, then my wife is divorced, he has used divorce to strengthen his resolve to do something, or to confirm a news in place of swearing by Allah, because for these confirmations swearing by Allah has been legislated, not divorce. Thus the man has done wrong by using divorce for other than what it was legislated for, and thus deviated from its legal objectives. Most divorce tragedies occur through conditional divorce hanging on their own actions or those of their wives or others, such as eating, drinking, coming, going, visiting, speaking to someone, wearing a garment, etc., for the sake of urging the action or preventing it, depending on whether the condition is negative or positive, like: if you do such and such, or if I do not do. Rather, they often suspend divorce on actions between them and their friends or enemies, in which their wives have no involvement. Page 249, Chapter Four: Derivation of Modern Codification from Fiqh. 4/18 - Some thinkers among contemporary scholars hold that the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be considered as one large madhhab in the Sharia, and each individual madhhab among them, like the Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali, and others, is considered in this large general madhhab as different opinions and statements within a single individual madhhab. Thus the scholars of the Ummah weigh and choose from them for codification in the field of judiciary and fatwa what is most fulfilling of the temporal need and the requirements of maslaha in every era. Key Terms

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Unified Madhhab Principle for Codification and Its Application to Family Law
Proposing a meta-madhhab principle and its application to divorce reform

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of treating all ijtihadi madhhabs as one school to justify modern legislation using istihsan or istislah, as exemplified by the prohibition of suspended divorce.

Core Idea
All ijtihadi madhhabs form one large school; legislators choose the most suitable opinion for time and maslaha.

This passage presents a bold methodological principle for modern fiqh: we can treat the entire corpus of ijtihadi madhhabs as a single school, selecting rulings that best meet contemporary needs. The author then applies this to the devastating social problem of suspended divorce, arguing that even if early madhhabs didn't explicitly prohibit it, istihsan or istislah justifies doing so. Finally, he cites the Syrian Personal Status Law as a practical example of this approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core qawida
Extract the principle: the set of ijtihadi madhhabs is considered one large school; each school is like a different opinion within that school.
This is a wusta-level maxim governing codification and fatwa.
2
Understand its scope and limit
Note that it applies primarily to muamalat and family law, not to ibadat (worship), which are tawqifi (revelation-based).
The exception for ibadat prevents talfiq that would break a rukn.
3
Apply to suspended divorce
Trace how the principle justifies the Egyptian measure: the ban on suspended divorce, even if not found in early schools, is adopted via istihsan/istislah to achieve maslaha.
This shows the principle's operational mechanism – choose the ruling that best fulfills temporal need.
4
Examine the Syrian law example
The 1953 Syrian Personal Status Law – authored by al-Zarqa – drew from all madhhabs and prior Ottoman reforms, embodying the unified school approach.
This confirms the principle's practical feasibility.

And some thinkers among contemporary scholars hold that the set of ijtihadi madhhabs should be considered as one large madhhab in the Sharia, and each individual madhhab among them, like the Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali, and others, is considered in this large general madhhab as different opinions and statements within a single individual madhhab. Thus the scholars of the Ummah weigh and choose from them for codification in the field of judiciary and fatwa what is most fulfilling of the temporal need and the requirements of maslaha in every era. This is a sound opinion. (1) ... in it, so the condition upon which it was suspended occurs, and the woman is surprised by a divorce that disrupts the family, having done nothing to deserve it nor having any knowledge of it. And the suspended divorce may be three, after which there is no possibility of return. All this originates from people's deviation in divorce from its legal style and purpose since ancient times, and their using it in a suspended form to strengthen resolve to do or leave something, instead of swearing by Allah, for other than what divorce was legislated for, even contrary to its legal purpose which the Quranic texts indicate its limits. Scholars are heedless of this and surrendered to people's whims and ignorance in changing the legitimate way in the most dangerous matter. The Egyptian country has gotten rid of these tragedies by prohibiting suspended divorce and nullifying it in judiciary if the intention behind its suspension was only urging or preventing (i.e., incitement to do or leave). This measure in Egypt, even if it had no basis in the old fiqh madhhabs that Ibn al-Qayyim transmitted in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in from senior Companions, it would have been incumbent upon today's scholars of the Ummah to adopt it by way of istihsan or istislah, to return people to the legitimate path in using divorce and to put an end to the tragedies of their ignorance in it. (1) Finally, in Syria in 1953 CE (after the third edition of this Fiqh Introduction), a unified personal status law was issued encompassing all family law rulings in its four sections: marriage and its branches: alimony, lineage, custody, divorce, separation, iddah, etc.; capacity and guardianship and its branches: legal representation for minors in all its forms - like guardianship, custodianship, etc.; will and its conditions and rulings; inheritance. And I was one of the members of the official committee entrusted by the Syrian government with its drafting. So we derived its rulings from Islamic fiqh with all its madhhabs and opinions, and we adopted all the reformative rulings that had been taken by the Ottoman Family Rights Law before, and the laws -

Commentary
This passage presents a key methodological principle for modern codification: considering all ijtihadi madhhabs as one large school, where individual madhhabs are treated like different opinions within a single school. This allows legislators and muftis to choose the opinion that best fulfills the needs of the time and maslaha. The author endorses this as a 'sound opinion' (ra’yun sadīd). The passage then returns to the divorce issue, explaining the negative consequences of conditional and triple divorce, and justifying the Egyptian prohibition even if it lacked early support; it could be adopted through istihsan or istislah. Finally, the footnote describes the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953, which the author helped draft, and which derived its rules from all fiqh madhhabs and previous Ottoman reforms. This exemplifies the practical application of the principle just stated.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): طريق من طرق الاجتهاد يعدل فيه المجتهد عن حكم القياس إلى حكم آخر أقوى دليلاً أو أحقق لمصلحة، ويستخدم هنا لتبرير تشريع حديث لم يرد في المذاهب الأربعة.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): الأخذ بالمصلحة التي لم ينص عليها الشرع، ويستخدم هنا كأساس لتشريع منع الطلاق المعلق.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Treating All Madhabs as One Source for Modern Codification
Advocacy for cross-madhhab selection in codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of selecting rulings from all madhabs for codification based on temporal needs and public interest, and evaluate the use of istihsan and istislah in reforming practices like suspended divorce.

Core Idea
All ijtihadi schools are one vast resource; select the best ruling for each age.

Contemporary scholar Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa argues that all ijtihadi madhabs should be treated as one unified source for modern codification. Scholars must select rulings that best fulfill temporal needs and public interest (maslahah). This approach was practically applied in the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953, which adopted reforms such as invalidating suspended divorce when used as an oath. The author justifies such reforms using principles like istihsan and istislah, demonstrating the flexibility of Islamic fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Grasp the author's idea that the set of ijtihadi madhabs (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali, etc.) should be considered as one great school. Individual schools are like different opinions within a single school.
This is not about mixing arbitrarily but about having a broader pool of opinions to choose from.
2
Identify the selection criteria
The selection for codification is based on 'al-hajah al-zamaniyyah' (temporal need) and 'muqtadayat al-maslahah' (requirements of public interest). Scholars weigh and choose the most suitable ruling.
The criteria are principled, not merely convenient or popular.
3
Analyze the suspended divorce example
The author critiques the misuse of suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq) as an oath. Egypt nullified it when used only for inducement or deterrence. This reform is justified by istihsan or istislah.
The author argues that even without classical precedent, this reform would be incumbent on scholars to protect families.
4
Recognize the tools of reform
Istihsan (juristic preference) and istislah (consideration of public interest) are the methodological tools that allow selecting a ruling that deviates from the literal analogy to achieve a better outcome.
These tools are well-established in Hanafi and Maliki usul.
5
Apply to the Syrian law
The Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953 is a model: it drew from all madhabs and included reforms like those on suspended divorce, triple divorce, duration of pregnancy, etc. This proves fiqh's capacity to respond to modern needs.
The author was part of the committee that drafted it.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh
4/18 - Some contemporary scholars think that the set of ijtihadi schools should be considered as one great school in Sharia, and each individual school such as Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali and others are regarded in this great general school like different opinions and statements within a single individual school, so the scholars of the Ummah weigh and choose from them for codification in the field of judiciary and fatwa what is most fulfilling of the temporal need and the requirements of public interest in every age. This is a sound opinion.
(1) [Note on suspended divorce] ... So the conditional act occurs and the woman is surprised by a divorce that disrupts the family; she was not guilty of its cause nor had knowledge of it. And the suspended divorce may be three [pronouncements] after which there is no reunion. All that originates from people's deviation from the legitimate style and purpose of divorce since ancient times, and their using it as a suspended [oath] to strengthen resolve to do or leave something, instead of swearing by Allah, in what is not the intended purpose of divorce, rather contrary to its legitimate goal indicated by Quranic texts. The scholars are heedless of this and surrender to people's folly and ignorance in changing the legitimate path in the most serious matter. Egypt has gotten rid of these tragedies by prohibiting suspended divorce and nullifying it judicially if its purpose was only inducement or deterrence (i.e., urging to do or leave). This measure in Egypt, even if it had no basis in the old fiqh schools transmitted by Ibn al-Qayyim in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in from senior Companions, would have been incumbent upon today's scholars to establish it by way of istihsan or istislah, returning people to the legitimate way in using divorce, and eliminating the tragedies of their ignorance therein.
(1) Finally, in Syria in 1953 CE (after the third edition of this fiqh introduction) a general personal status law was issued covering all family rulings in its four chapters: marriage and its derivatives (maintenance, lineage, custody, divorce, separation, waiting period, etc.), legal capacity and guardianship and its derivatives (legal representation for minors in all forms - tutorship, curatorship, etc.), will and its conditions and rulings, and inheritance. I was one of the members of the official committee entrusted by the Syrian government to draft it. We derived its rulings from Islamic fiqh in all its schools and opinions, and we adopted all the reformist rulings previously adopted by the Ottoman Law of Family Rights and the scattered Egyptian laws dealing with various personal status issues we mentioned earlier, such as the case of the missing husband's wife, suspended divorce and triple divorce, the duration of pregnancy, inheritance of siblings with grandfather and the common problem (see 10/4), the rule of inheritance of distant relatives, the famous case of the deprived son, the obligatory bequest, etc. So this law of ours was the first complete law of its kind in the entire Islamic world in its comprehensive codification of all personal status rulings and what it contained of reformist rulings and enlightened progressive steps within the path of Sharia and its limits. This law in its subject came as the best practical proof of the sufficiency and ability of Islamic fiqh in its broad sense in its various schools and principles to respond to various temporal legislative needs. We have already referred in the discussion of istislah to this law and that it repealed and replaced the Ottoman Law of Family Rights (see 12/5 footnote). We will refer in an appendix to this chapter to two recent projects for codifying personal status from Islamic fiqh in its various schools without restricting to a single school.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the author's legal philosophy regarding modern codification from Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, argues that all ijtihadi schools (madhhabs) should be treated as one unified source of law, where the opinions of different schools are akin to different opinions within a single school. This approach allows legislators and jurists to select the most suitable rulings for the needs of the time and public interest (maslahah). The author then provides a practical example: the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953, which he helped draft. This law drew from all madhhabs and earlier reformist laws, addressing issues like suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq), triple divorce, and inheritance problems. The author uses the example of suspended divorce to illustrate how the law can correct prevalent misuse of divorce by prohibiting it when used merely as an oath (to compel or deter), a measure supported by istihsan (juristic preference) or istislah (consideration of public interest). The passage underscores the flexibility and comprehensiveness of Islamic fiqh for contemporary legislation.

Key Terms
استمداد التقنين المعاصر (istimdad al-taqnin al-mu'asir): The process of deriving modern codified laws from classical Islamic fiqh, utilizing all schools of thought.
المذاهب الاجتهادية (al-madhahib al-ijtihadiyyah): The schools of Islamic jurisprudence that are based on independent reasoning (ijtihad), such as Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali.
الطلاق المعلق (al-talaq al-mu'allaq): Divorce that is made conditional upon the occurrence of a future event, often used as an oath.

The Syrian Personal Status Law: A Model of Modern Fiqh Codification
Describing the Syrian law of 1953 as the first comprehensive codification of Islamic personal status law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the significance of the Syrian Personal Status Law as an example of modern codification of Islamic fiqh and identify how it demonstrates the adaptability of fiqh to contemporary needs while staying within Sharia.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh is not static; modern codification proves it can meet contemporary needs while staying within Sharia.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa presents the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953 as a landmark achievement in the modern history of Islamic law. It is described as the first comprehensive codification of personal status in the Islamic world, drawing from multiple madhhabs rather than a single school. The author argues that this law serves as practical proof that Islamic fiqh, in its broad and diverse sense, is sufficient and adaptable to respond to changing legislative needs, thus rebutting the notion that codification would rigidify the law.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the legal shift
Identify the previous legal regime (the Ottoman Law of Family Rights, based solely on Hanafi fiqh) and how the Syrian law replaced it. Note the author's mention that the Syrian law abrogated the Ottoman code.
The Ottoman law was purely Hanafi; the Syrian law is multi-madhhab.
2
Identify the central claim
Recognize the author's claim: the Syrian law is the first complete codification of personal status in the Islamic world, and it demonstrates the sufficiency and adaptability of fiqh across madhhabs to meet temporal legislative needs.
The phrase 'خير برهان عملي' (best practical proof) is key.
3
Analyze the evidence
List the specific problematic cases the law addressed: missing husband, suspended/triple divorce, duration of pregnancy, inheritance with grandfather, distant relatives, and the obligatory bequest. These show the law tackled known issues in fiqh.
These cases were previously dealt with in scattered laws; the Syrian law unified them.
4
Understand the methodology
The law uses a multi-madhhab approach ('من الفقه الإسلامي بمختلف مذاهبه') rather than confining itself to one school. This is a shift from the Ottoman precedent and reflects the modern trend of talfiq and tarjih across schools.
This methodology is sometimes called 'fiqh al-muqaranah' (comparative fiqh) in action.
5
Evaluate the implications for iftaa
Consider how this model affects the mufti's task: the mufti no longer must adhere strictly to one madhhab but may adopt rulings from other schools to achieve justice and public interest (maslahah), provided the ruling stays within the boundaries of Sharia.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle of 'al-hukm yadur ma'a al-illah' and the use of istihsan.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the modern trend in utilizing Islamic fiqh for codification, specifically highlighting the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents this law as a practical demonstration that Islamic fiqh, in its broad sense encompassing multiple madhhabs and usul, is sufficient and adaptable to meet contemporary legislative needs. The law addressed various problematic cases such as the missing husband, suspended and triple divorce, duration of pregnancy, inheritance with the grandfather, and the obligatory bequest. The author claims this law was the first comprehensive codification of personal status in the Islamic world. He earlier mentioned that this law replaced the Ottoman Law of Family Rights (which had been applied in Syria). The author also promises to mention two modern codification projects that draw from multiple madhhabs, not just one, in the appendix.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnīn): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh rulings into statutory law
الأحوال الشخصية (al-aḥwāl al-shakhṣiyyah): Personal status law covering marriage, divorce, custody, maintenance, inheritance, etc.
قانون حقوق العائلة العثماني (qānūn ḥuqūq al-ʿāʾilah al-ʿuthmānī): The Ottoman Law of Family Rights, a codification based on Hanafi fiqh that was replaced by the Syrian law
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): The principle of public interest (maslahah) as a source of law, often used to justify codification and legal reform

A) It was the first law to include provisions for inheritance.
B) It was the first complete codification of personal status in the Islamic world, drawn from multiple madhhabs.

The Value of Juristic Differences in Fiqh
Defense of juristic diversity in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the Hanafi view on the value of juristic disagreement and distinguish between harmful differences (creed) and beneficial differences (fiqh).

Core Idea
Juristic differences in fiqh are a legislative treasure, not a defect; they provide flexibility and alternative solutions.

Many assume that multiple madhhabs indicate confusion or contradiction in Islamic law. This chapter refutes that illusion, explaining that differences in practical rulings are a source of richness and adaptability. In the Hanafi tradition, such ikhtilaf is welcomed as a mercy and a means to address diverse circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Locate the opening sentences where the author describes the mistaken belief that differences in ijtihad are a defect or contradiction.
This sets up the refutation.
2
Distinguish between two types of ikhtilaf
Note the sharp contrast: differences in creed (aqidah) are harmful, while differences in fiqh are praiseworthy and beneficial.
The author uses 'blameworthy' (shā'in) for creedal differences and 'treasure' (dhakhā'ir) for fiqh differences.
3
Analyze the rational benefits
Read the paragraph detailing why multiple legal theories and methods are valuable: they provide legislative wealth, adapt to changing needs, and offer alternative solutions.
Focus on the phrases 'tharwah tashrī'iyyah' (legislative wealth) and 'tawfur lahā ususan ṣāliḥah' (provides sound foundations).
4
Apply the analogies
Examine the two analogies: seats of different heights and multiple pharmacies. Explain how each illustrates the value of having multiple valid options.
The analogies show that variety allows for comfort (choice) and access (solutions).
5
Connect to fatwa practice
Consider how this principle justifies following a different madhhab in case of hardship or necessity, which is a key aspect of Hanafi fatwa methodology.
This links the philosophical argument to practical iftā'.

الفصل التاسع عشر
ضرورة اختلاف الاجتهادات وقيمته
۱/۱۹ - بعض المتوهمين ممن لا علم عندهم ولا بصيرة لهم يظنون أن اختلاف الاجتهادات في الفقه الإسلامي نقيصة، ويتمنون لو لم يكن إلا مذهب واحد.
وقد يذهبون إلى أبعد من هذا في أوهامهم فيرون أن اختلاف المذاهب قد تناقضاً يوهم في المصدر التشريعي!
ودفعاً لهذا الوهم الفاسد نقول : إنَّ الاختلاف المذهبي الشائن المستكره الذي ليس له في الأمة إلا سيئات الآثار إنما هو الاختلاف في العقائد .
أما الاختلاف الفقهي في الأحكام العملية المدنية فهو من المفاخر والذخائر، لأنه ثروة تشريعية كلما اتسعت كانت أروع وأنفع وأنجع. فإن معنى هذا الاختلاف تعدد النظريات والمبادىء والطرائق هو الحقوقية في استمداد الأحكام وتقريرها. وهذا يجعل الأمة في غنى من تشريعها لا يضيق بها عن حاجاتها. ويوفر لها أسساً صالحة لحل المشكلات العارضة باختلاف الظروف ويفتح مجالاً لاختيار الحلول الأفضل كلما دعت الحاجة وأظهر التطبيق بعض المشكلات. وشأنها في ذلك شأن له في بيته مقاعد من من مختلف الأوضاع والارتفاع، فإذا تعب في قعوده على أحدها استراح بالانتقال إلى سواه. أو كمن في بلده صيدليات كثيرة متفاوتة وكلها مستوفية للشرائط، فإذا لم يجد علاجه المطلوب في إحداها وجده في أخرى.
٢٦٩
Chapter Nineteen
The Necessity and Value of Differences in Ijtihad
1/19 - Some deluded people who have no knowledge or insight think that differences in ijtihad in Islamic jurisprudence are a defect, and they wish there were only one school.
They may go further in their illusions and see that differences among schools constitute a contradiction that casts doubt on the legislative source!
To refute this corrupt illusion, we say: Indeed, the blameworthy, objectionable sectarian difference that brings only evil consequences to the nation is only difference in beliefs.
As for juristic difference in practical civil rulings, it is a source of pride and treasure, because it is a legislative wealth—the more extensive it is, the more splendid, beneficial, and effective it becomes. For the meaning of this difference is the multiplicity of theories, principles, and legal methods in deriving and establishing rulings. This enriches the nation with its legislation, not falling short of its needs. It provides sound foundations for solving incidental problems arising from varying circumstances and opens the field for choosing the best solutions whenever need arises and application reveals some problems. Its situation in this regard is like that of a person who has in his house seats of various positions and heights; if he becomes tired from sitting on one, he rests by moving to another. Or like one who has in his country many pharmacies, varied and all fulfilling the conditions; if he does not find his required medicine in one, he finds it in another.
269
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a chapter on the necessity and value of differences in ijtihad. The author refutes the misconception that juristic disagreement is a flaw. He distinguishes between differences in 'aqidah (creed), which are harmful, and differences in fiqh (practical rulings), which are a legislative treasure. The latter provides the Muslim community with a wealth of legal theories and principles, enabling flexibility to meet changing needs and circumstances. The author uses two analogies: seats of different heights for comfort, and multiple pharmacies offering varied medicines. In the Hanafi tradition, this principle supports the acceptance of multiple valid opinions within the madhhab and the broader Islamic legal heritage, allowing for the selection of the most suitable ruling when necessary.

Key Terms
اختلاف الاجتهادات (ikhtilaf al-ijtihadat): Disagreement among qualified jurists in deriving legal rulings from primary sources.
الاختلاف المذهبي (al-ikhtilaf al-madhhabi): Difference between one school of thought and another in fiqh rulings and methods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between differences in beliefs and differences in fiqh?
A) Both are equally harmful.
B) Differences in beliefs are harmful, while differences in fiqh are a treasure.
C) Differences in fiqh are harmful, while differences in beliefs are a treasure.
D) Neither has any value.

Key Takeaway
Differences in fiqh are a legislative wealth that provides flexibility and alternative solutions; they are not a defect.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Value of Juristic Disagreement in Fiqh
Refutation of misconception about ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why juristic disagreement in subsidiary rulings (furu‘) is considered a mercy in the Hanafi tradition, and apply this understanding to evaluate common objections against madhhab diversity.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf in furu‘ is a legislative treasure, not a defect.

Many laypeople and even some students mistakenly view the existence of multiple madhhabs as a sign of weakness or contradiction in Islamic law. This lesson refutes that misconception by drawing a sharp line between blameworthy disagreement in creed (usul al-din) and praiseworthy diversity in practical rulings (furu‘). Using the hadith of the mujtahid judge, the practice of the Companions, and statements of early imams, the author builds a case for ikhtilaf as a mercy and a source of flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish creedal from juristic disagreement
Identify the author's key distinction: blameworthy ikhtilaf is in creed ('aqa'id); juristic ikhtilaf in furu‘ is praiseworthy. This is the foundation of the argument.
Creedal disagreement leads to division; juristic disagreement enriches the Shari'ah.
2
Follow the refutation of the false view
The author refutes those who see ikhtilaf as a defect. They misunderstand Quran 3:103 ('do not become divided'), thinking it forbids all difference. The author explains the verse refers to creedal division or mutual accusation of error.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith 'When a judge exercises ijtihad and is correct, he has two rewards; if he errs, one reward' is cited. This shows that even incorrect ijtihad is rewarded, and the mujtahid's opinion is not sinful. This is a direct dalil for the legitimacy of ikhtilaf.
The hadith is mutafaq 'alayh (agreed upon).
4
Examine the historical evidence
Imam Malik's response to Harun al-Rashid and statements of Ibn al-Arabi and Shatibi demonstrate that the early community accepted and valued difference. The Companions differed and dispersed, and each considered himself correct.
This shows that ikhtilaf is a practical necessity, not a flaw.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that the Ummah enjoys flexibility. A mufti may choose among valid opinions to ease hardship, like a person shifting chairs or finding medicine in a different pharmacy.
This principle is used in contemporary fatwa and codification.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
فيه
فكذلك حال المذاهب الفقهية في التشريع الإسلامي.
ونقل السيوطي في مناقب الإمام مالك» (ص) (٤٦) قول مالك : «شاورني هارون الرشيد في .. أن يعلق الموطأ في الكعبة، ويحمل الناس على ما فقلت يا أمير المؤمنين .. إنَّ أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اختلفوا في الفروع فافترقوا في البلدان، وكلّ عند نفسه مصيب . . ) ويبدو أن الرشيد كرر ، عليه هذا الطلب، فقال مالك: يا أمير المؤمنين، إنَّ اختلاف العلماء رحمة الله على هذه الأمة كل يتبع ما صح عنده، وكل على هدى، وكل يريد الله (نقلاً عن كتاب «مالك» لأبي زهرة ، ص ۱۹۱ - ۱۹۲).
من
وقال أبو بكر بن العربي المالكي، توفي ٥٤٣ هـ) في كتابه الأحكام الصغرى ١٥٣/١ ، تحقيق سعيد أغراب منشورات المنظمة الإسلامية للتربية والعلوم والثقافة - ايسيسكو، ١٤١٢ هـ - ١٩٩١ م). في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاعْتَصِمُوا بِحَبْلِ اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا وَلَا تَفَرَّقُوا .. (آل عمران (۱۰۳۲):
:
ولا تفرقوا يعني في العقائد وقيل : لا تحاسدوا . . وقيل : المراد التخطئة في الفروع، أي لا يخطىء أحدكم ،صاحبه، وليمض كل واحد على اجتهاده، فإن الكل معتصم بحبل الله ،بدليله والتفرق المنهي عنه هو ما أدى إلى الفتنة والتشتيت وأما الاختلاف في الفروع فهو من محاسن الشريعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: إذا اجتهد الحاكم فأصاب فله أجران : وإذا اجتهد فأخطأ فله أجرٌ واحد» [رواه الشيخان وسواهما من ائمة الحديث].

ويقول الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي في كتابه «الاعتصام» (۱۱/۳) ما
روى ابن وهب عن القاسم بن محمد أيضاً، قال:
أعجبني قول عمر بن عبد العزيز:
ما أحب أن أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يختلفون، لأنه لو كان قولاً
٢٧٠
Chapter Nineteen: The Necessity and Value of Differences in Ijtihad. 1/19 - Some deluded people who have no knowledge or insight imagine that differences of ijtihad in Islamic jurisprudence are a defect, and they wish there were only one school. They may go even further in their illusions, thinking that the difference of schools implies a contradiction in the legislative source! To refute this false illusion, we say: The blameworthy, detested sectarian difference that brings only evil consequences to the Ummah is only difference in creed. As for juristic difference in practical civil rulings, it is a source of pride and treasure, because it is a legislative wealth—the broader it becomes, the more splendid, useful, and effective it is. For the meaning of this difference is the multiplicity of theories, principles, and legal methods in deriving and establishing rulings. This makes the Ummah self-sufficient in its legislation, not constrained by its needs. It provides sound foundations for solving emerging problems due to changing circumstances, and opens the field for choosing the best solutions whenever need arises and application reveals some problems. Its situation in this regard is like that of a person who has in his house chairs of various shapes and heights; if he tires of sitting on one, he rests by moving to another. Or like one who in his country has many pharmacies of varying quality, all meeting conditions; if he does not find his required medicine in one, he finds it in another. 269. Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh. So likewise is the case of the juristic schools in Islamic legislation. Al-Suyuti narrated in 'Manaqib al-Imam Malik' (p. 46) the saying of Malik: 'Harun al-Rashid consulted me about hanging al-Muwatta' on the Ka'bah and compelling the people to follow it... I said, O Commander of the Faithful, the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) differed in subsidiary matters and dispersed in the lands, and each considered himself correct...' It seems al-Rashid repeated this request to him, so Malik said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, the difference of scholars is a mercy from Allah upon this Ummah; each follows what is authentic with him, each is guided, and each seeks Allah.' (Quoted from the book 'Malik' by Abu Zahra, pp. 191-192). And Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki (d. 543 AH) said in his book 'al-Ahkam al-Sughra' (1/153, ed. by Sa'id A'rab, ISESCO, 1412/1991) regarding the verse: 'And hold firmly to the rope of Allah all together and do not become divided' (Al 'Imran 3:103): 'Do not become divided' means in creeds; it is also said: do not envy one another; and it is said: the intended meaning is refraining from accusing one another of error in subsidiary matters—that is, do not declare your companion mistaken, and let each one proceed according to his ijtihad, for all are holding to the rope of Allah by their evidence. And the prohibited division is that which leads to discord and scattering. As for difference in subsidiary matters, it is among the beauties of the Shari'ah because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'If a judge exercises ijtihad and is correct, he has two rewards; if he exercises ijtihad and errs, he has one reward' [narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim and other imams of hadith]. And Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi says in his book 'al-I'tisam' (1/3) the following text: Ibn Wahb narrated from al-Qasim ibn Muhammad also, he said: I was pleased by the statement of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: I would not like that the Companions of Muhammad (peace be upon him) had not differed, because if there were only one saying... 270
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a common misconception: that differences of legal opinion (ikhtilaf al-ijtihadat) are a defect in Islamic law. The author begins by identifying those who hold this view as lacking knowledge and insight. He then draws a sharp distinction between two types of difference: (1) blameworthy difference in matters of creed ('aqa'id), which brings only evil, and (2) praiseworthy difference in juristic rulings (ahkam 'amaliyyah madaniyyah), which is a source of pride and legislative wealth. This second type represents a multiplicity of theories, principles, and legal methods that enrich the Shari'ah. The author offers two analogies: a person with many chairs of different shapes who can shift when tired, and a city with many pharmacies where if one lacks a medicine, another may have it. He then supports his argument with historical reports. First, he quotes Imam Malik's response to Harun al-Rashid when the caliph wanted to make the Muwatta' the sole authoritative text: the Companions differed in subsidiary matters and spread across lands, each considering himself correct; later, Malik explicitly says that difference of scholars is a mercy. Then he cites Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi's commentary on verse 3:103, explaining that 'do not become divided' refers to creeds or to refraining from mutual accusations of error in subsidiary matters, and that the forbidden division is that which causes discord. He cites the hadith of the mujtahid judge (if correct two rewards, if mistaken one reward) as evidence that ikhtilaf in furu' is a beauty of the Shari'ah. Finally, he begins a quote from al-Shatibi and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, which is cut off, but the gist is that difference among the Companions is beneficial because if there were only one opinion, the Ummah would be in difficulty. The overall message is that juristic diversity is a mercy and a practical necessity, not a flaw.

Key Terms
اختلاف الاجتهادات (ikhtilāf al-ijtihādāt): Differences in legal reasoning and opinions among qualified jurists (mujtahids) regarding subsidiary rulings (furu').
فروع (furū‘): Subsidiary or practical legal rulings that are not part of fundamental beliefs (usul al-din).
اجتهاد (ijtihād): The exertion of mental effort by a qualified jurist to derive a ruling from the primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is juristic disagreement considered a 'legislative wealth'?
A) It proves the weakness of the Quran and Sunnah as sources.
B) It provides multiple theories and methods to address changing circumstances.
C) It was desired by Harun al-Rashid to unify the Ummah.
D) It prevents any single scholar from being correct.

Key Takeaway
Juristic disagreement in subsidiary rulings (furu') is a mercy and legislative wealth that provides flexibility and solutions for the Ummah across time and circumstances.

8 min
The Mercy of Ikhtilaf in Fiqh
Defending ikhtilaf in furu' as mercy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the classical proofs that ikhtilaf in furu' is a mercy and evaluate its role as a strength of Islamic law.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf among scholars in subsidiary rulings is a divine mercy, not a defect—it provides flexibility and rewards even mistaken ijtihad.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi assembles key classical arguments establishing that juristic difference (ikhtilaf) is not a sign of confusion but a blessing. We will examine proofs from the Companions' practice, the hadith of the mujtahid, and statements of early Imams, grounding the Hanafi acceptance of ikhtilaf in furu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Determine the thesis being defended: that ikhtilaf al-furu' (difference in subsidiary rulings) is a mercy and a necessary feature of Islamic law, not a flaw.
The author uses the phrase 'رحمة الله على هذه الأمة' (mercy from Allah upon this nation).
2
Examine the first proof: Imam Malik's refusal
Analyze Malik's response to Harun al-Rashid: citing the Companions' own differences and dispersion as precedent against imposing a single text. Each Companion, according to Malik, was correct in his own view.
This establishes that even the earliest generation had legitimate differences.
3
Examine the second proof: Ibn al-Arabi's tafsir and the hadith
See how Ibn al-Arabi interprets 'do not become divided' as referring to beliefs or envy, not fiqh differences. The hadith of the mujtahid (correct=2 rewards, mistaken=1 reward) shows that ijtihad is rewarded even if wrong, so difference is not prohibited.
The hadith is cited as dalil for the validity of ikhtilaf in furu'.
4
Examine the third proof: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's statement
Understand Umar's reasoning: uniformity would cause hardship (ḍīq), while multiple opinions provide capacity (saʿah). The existence of ikhtilaf is a mercy that eases the community.
Al-Shatibi uses this to argue that the Companions opened the door of ijtihad.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the proofs: ikhtilaf is a necessary result of sincere ijtihad, sharpens minds, and enriches fiqh. It is not a defect but a strength, comparable to diversity in Roman legal heritage.
The author contrasts Western pride in legal diversity with Muslims sometimes undervaluing their own rich ikhtilaf.

ولا تفرقوا يعني في العقائد وقيل : لا تحاسدوا . . وقيل : المراد التخطئة في الفروع، أي لا يخطىء أحدكم ،صاحبه، وليمض كل واحد على اجتهاده، فإن الكل معتصم بحبل الله ،بدليله والتفرق المنهي عنه هو ما أدى إلى الفتنة والتشتيت وأما الاختلاف في الفروع فهو من محاسن الشريعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: إذا اجتهد الحاكم فأصاب فله أجران : وإذا اجتهد فأخطأ فله أجرٌ واحد» [رواه الشيخان وسواهما من ائمة الحديث].

ويقول الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي في كتابه «الاعتصام» (۱۱/۳) ما
روى ابن وهب عن القاسم بن محمد أيضاً، قال:
أعجبني قول عمر بن عبد العزيز:
ما أحب أن أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يختلفون، لأنه لو كان قولاً

الفصل (۱۹) ضرورة اختلاف الاجتهادات وقيمته
واحداً لكان الناس في ضيق وإنهم أئمة يُقتدى بهم، فلو أخذ رجل بقول أحدهم لكان في سعة».
ومعنى هذا أنهم فتحوا للناس باب الاجتهاد وجواز الاختلاف فيه، لأنهم لو لم يفتحوه لكان المجتهدون في ضيق فوسع الله على الأمة بوجود الخلاف الفروعي فيهم فكان فتح باب للدخول في هذه الرحمة انتهى كلام الشاطبي . ويقول الأستاذ محمد أبو زهرة تعليقاً على ذلك :
«لقد كان اختلاف الصحابة في الفروع رائده الإخلاص. ولذا لم يكن بينهم تنازع في الفقه ولا تعصب، بل طلب للحقيقة وبحث عن الصواب من أي ناحية أخذ ومن أي جهة استبان.
وإن ذلك الاختلاف كان فيه شحذ للأذهان، واستخراج للأحكم من القرآن؛ واستنباط قانون شرعي عام وإن لم يكن مسطوراً... ونحن لا نرى الخلاف في الفروع، إلا ثمرات ناضجة لما بنّه القرآن الكريم والسنة النبوية في نفوس الناس من البحث بعقولهم وتدبير شؤونهم بالشورى ومبادلة الرأي، مستضيئين بسنة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ومستظلين بأحكام القرآن» اهـ . ر: مقدمة كتاب الملكية ونظرية العقد للأستاذ أبي زهرة؛ ف/ ۱۳/
ص ۱۹ - ۲۰) .
هذا، وإننا نرى اليوم علماء الحقوق من الإفرنج يتباهون بما انحدر إليهم في تراثهم التشريعي عن الرومانيين من آراء ونظريات مختلفة مأثورة عن فقهاء الشرع الروماني وشُرَّاحه ولا يعدون تلك الآراء المختلفة من النقائص أو النقائض التي يتمنون أن لا تكون في ميراثهم القانوني، بينما لا يعد الفقه الروماني على سعته إلا شيئاً يسيراً، وغيضاً من فيض بالنسبة إلى ما أورثتناه الاجتهادات من فقه الشريعة الإسلامية.
٢/١٩ - وهذا الاختلاف الفقهي نتيجة ضرورية لا يمكن أن لا تكون
ما دام للعلماء نظرات لا تتحد، وأفهام لا تتفق.
٢٧١
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh. Therein: Thus is the state of the juristic schools in Islamic legislation. Al-Suyuti narrated in 'Manaqib al-Imam Malik' (p. 46) the saying of Malik: 'Harun al-Rashid consulted me about... hanging al-Muwatta' in the Ka'bah and compelling people to follow what I said. I said, O Commander of the Faithful... The Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) differed in subsidiary matters and dispersed in the lands, and each, in his own view, is correct...' It seems al-Rashid repeated this request to him, so Malik said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, the difference of scholars is a mercy from Allah upon this nation. Each follows what is authentic to him, each is guided, and each seeks Allah' (quoted from the book 'Malik' by Abu Zahra, pp. 191-192). And Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki (d. 543 AH) said in his book 'al-Ahkam al-Sughra' (1/153, ed. Sa'id A'rab, ISESCO, 1991) regarding the verse: 'And hold fast to the rope of Allah all together and do not become divided' (Aal Imran 103): 'Do not become divided' means in beliefs, and it is said: do not envy one another... and it is said: the intent is [prohibiting] declaring error in subsidiary matters, i.e., one should not declare his companion wrong, and each should proceed according to his ijtihad, for all are holding fast to the rope of Allah through their evidence. The prohibited division is that which leads to discord and dispersion. As for difference in subsidiary matters, it is among the beauties of the Shari'ah because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'When a judge exercises ijtihad and is correct, he has two rewards; and when he exercises ijtihad and errs, he has one reward' [narrated by the two Shaykhs and others]. Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi says in his book 'al-I'tisam' (1/3): Ibn Wahb narrated from al-Qasim ibn Muhammad also, he said: 'I liked the statement of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz: I do not wish that the Companions of Muhammad (peace be upon him) had not differed, because if there were only one opinion, people would be in difficulty. And they are imams to be followed, so if a person takes the opinion of any one of them, he is in ample capacity.' The meaning is that they opened for people the door of ijtihad and allowed difference therein, because if they had not opened it, the mujtahids would be in difficulty. So Allah eased for the nation by the existence of subsidiary difference among them, thus opening a door to enter into this mercy. End of al-Shatibi's words. Professor Muhammad Abu Zahra commented: 'The difference of the Companions in subsidiary matters was motivated by sincerity. Hence there was neither conflict in jurisprudence nor fanaticism among them, but rather a search for truth and investigation of correctness from whichever direction it was taken and from whichever source it became clear. That difference sharpened minds, extracted the most profound judgments from the Qur'an, and deduced a general legal law even if not written... We do not see difference in subsidiary matters except as ripe fruits of what the Noble Qur'an and Prophetic Sunnah implanted in the souls of people: searching with their intellects, managing their affairs through consultation and exchange of opinion, illuminated by the Sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and sheltered by the rulings of the Qur'an.' End quote. From the introduction to the book 'al-Milkiyyah wa Nazariyyah al-Aqd' by Abu Zahra; part 13, pp. 19-20. Today, we see Western legal scholars boasting about the various opinions and theories descended to them in their legal heritage from the Romans, attributed to Roman jurists and commentators, and they do not consider those differing views as defects or contradictions that they would wish not to be in their legal inheritance. Yet Roman jurisprudence, despite its breadth, is but a small thing, a drop in the ocean compared to what the ijtihadats have bequeathed to us from the fiqh of Islamic Shari'ah. 2/19 - This juristic difference is a necessary result that cannot but exist as long as scholars have views that do not unify and understandings that do not agree.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This is a legal philosophy passage discussing the nature and value of ikhtilaf (difference) in furu' al-fiqh (subsidiary rulings). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents a series of classical arguments to establish that ikhtilaf is not a flaw but a mercy and a necessity. The passage begins by quoting Imam Malik's refusal to have al-Muwatta' imposed as a single authoritative text, citing the Companions' own differences as a precedent. Then Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi interprets the verse 'do not become divided' as applying to beliefs, not fiqh differences, and uses the hadith of the mujtahid's reward to show that even mistaken ijtihad is rewarded. Al-Shatibi quotes Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's statement that uniformity would cause hardship, so the existence of multiple valid opinions is a divine mercy. Muhammad Abu Zahra adds that the Companions' differences were sincere and productive, sharpening minds and extracting legal rulings. The author concludes by noting that Western scholars value diversity in their legal heritage, so Islamic fiqh, with its vast ijtihad-based richness, should be even more appreciated. The passage underscores that fiqh differences are inevitable for human minds approaching texts with varying understandings, and they are a source of flexibility, not a weakness.

Key Terms
اختلاف الفروع (ikhtilāf al-furūʿ): Difference among jurists in subsidiary (non-fundamental) legal rulings derived from ijtihad.
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Independent reasoning to derive a ruling from the sources. The hadith about the judge shows that even a mistaken ijtihad is rewarded.
رحمة (raḥmah): Mercy; here, the difference among scholars is described as a mercy from Allah for the community, providing flexibility and capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz not wish that the Companions had not differed?
A) Because difference leads to conflict and should be avoided.
B) Because if there were only one opinion, people would be in difficulty (ḍīq).
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade any difference among Muslims.
D) Because the Romans had a unified legal system.

Key Takeaway
Ikhtilaf al-furu' is a divine mercy that provides flexibility, rewards ijtihad, and enriches Islamic law; it is not a defect but a strength.

Ikhtilaf as a Necessary Mercy
Philosophical justification of ikhtilaf as mercy and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why early scholars considered ikhtilaf in subsidiary matters a mercy and a necessity, and evaluate the view that it indicates inconsistency in Sharia.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf is not a defect but a mercy and a sign of Sharia's flexibility.

This lesson explores the philosophical foundation of juristic disagreement. Al-Zarqa, quoting al-Shatibi and Abu Zahra, argues that differing ijtihads are not only permissible but necessary for the ease of the community. Understanding this perspective is crucial for a mufti to appreciate the richness of the Hanafi tradition and to avoid dismissing legitimate differences as errors.

Guided Path
1
Examine the core claim
Identify the author's main thesis: ikhtilaf is necessary and valuable, not a defect.
2
Analyze al-Shatibi's reasoning
Understand the argument that opening the door of ijtihad prevents difficulty for people. The existence of ikhtilaf is a mercy.
Focus on 'saving from tightness' as a rationale.
3
Consider Abu Zahra's perspective
Note that the Companions' differences were motivated by sincerity and led to intellectual sharpening and extraction of rulings.
Example: differing rulings on inheritance cases based on sincere ijtihad.
4
Compare with Roman legal tradition
Contrast the Islamic acceptance of ikhtilaf with Western legal heritage which also values differing opinions, but Islamic fiqh is vastly richer.
5
Draw implications for a mufti
A mufti must recognize that ikhtilaf is a natural feature; reliance on tarjih and contextual analysis is key.
The mufta bihi is the strongest view in the Hanafi hierarchy.

لا يدل على تناقض في المصدر التشريعي المستنبط منه، كما لا تدل اختلافات آراء المحاكم في معنى مادة قانونية على تناقض فيها؛ وإنما يدل على مرونة النص، وسعة قابليته التطبيقية .
